## Agent 4 / D — Folded main/dual arithmetic invariant search

Executing the assigned Agent 4 task: one deeper pass for a **concrete folded main/dual invariant** that could prove incidence, rather than merely improve symbol rank. The current prompt asks specifically for pure-main, pure-dual, and mixed phase decomposition, candidate invariants, branch-separation analysis, the \(\alpha Q\) split, and a verdict. 

---

# 1. Decomposition of the Wronskian pairing

At a zero

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,
\]

the ordinary packet-frequency Wronskian satisfies

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)
=
\langle \mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle.
\]

Write the main/dual decomposition:

\[
\mathbf z'
=
\mathbf z'_+
+
\mathbf z'_-
+
\mathbf z'_{\Gamma}
+
\mathbf z'_E,
\]

\[
\mathbf M
=
\mathbf M_+
+
\mathbf M_-
+
\mathbf M_{\mathrm{mix}}
+
\mathbf M_{\Gamma}
+
\mathbf M_E.
\]

Then

\[
\langle \mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle
=
\langle \mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_+\rangle
+
\langle \mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_-\rangle
+
\langle \mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_-\rangle
+
\langle \mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_+\rangle
\]

\[
+
\langle \mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_{\mathrm{mix}}\rangle
+
\langle \mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_{\mathrm{mix}}\rangle
+
\text{background/error terms}.
\]

The phase families are:

\[
e^{-im\log N_+},
\]

\[
e^{+im\log N_-},
\]

\[
e^{-im\log(N_+/N_-)},
\]

and, with folded gamma phase,

\[
e^{ik\Theta(m)}e^{-im\log(N_+/N_-)}
\]

or variants with several folded dual factors.

Here

\[
N_+=\prod_{n\in S_+}n,
\qquad
N_-=\prod_{n\in S_-}n.
\]

Thus the exact object is a folded subset-product exponential sum over pure and mixed frequencies.

---

# 2. Candidate invariants

## 2.1 Skew invariant

Define

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm skew}
=
\operatorname{Im}
\langle\mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_-\rangle
-
\operatorname{Im}
\langle\mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_+\rangle.
\]

This tries to detect an orientation mismatch between the main and dual branches.

Problem: at a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero, one can have the branch balance

\[
Z_+\approx -Z_-.
\]

Then pairings of the form

\[
\overline{Z_+}Z_-
\]

are approximately real and negative, so the imaginary skew term can vanish. The same obstruction persists at derivative/cofactor level unless there is a separate phase-rotation law for \(\mathbf M_\pm\). No such law is visible.

So \(\mathcal I_{\rm skew}\) is not sign-protected.

---

## 2.2 Asymmetry invariant

Define

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm asym}
=
\langle \mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_+\rangle
-
\omega(m,\alpha)
\langle \mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_-\rangle,
\]

where \(\omega\) is chosen from the predicted gamma/folded AFE ratio.

This can be useful if one branch dominates or if the two branch amplitudes have incompatible scaling.

In packet-frequency coordinates,

\[
x^+=\lambda\log n\asymp c,
\]

\[
x^-=\lambda\log\frac{m}{2\pi n}\asymp1-c.
\]

For

\[
0<c<c_1<\frac12,
\]

the branches are separated:

\[
|x^+-x^-|\asymp 1-2c.
\]

This gives rank separation, but not necessarily amplitude dominance. If the AFE weights are balanced, \(\omega\) can make the two large branch terms cancel to high order.

So \(\mathcal I_{\rm asym}\) is not intrinsically coercive.

---

## 2.3 Filtered commutator

Let \(L_+\) be a polynomial filter in \(\mathcal D\) localized to \(x^+\asymp c\), and \(L_-\) localized to \(x^-\asymp1-c\). Since the branch gap is \(O(1)\), such filters can be built with degree \(O(R)\) and cost

\[
\exp(O(R\log R))=Q^{O(1)}.
\]

Define

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm comm}
=
\langle L_+\mathbf z',L_-\mathbf M\rangle
-
\langle L_-\mathbf z',L_+\mathbf M\rangle.
\]

This is the best candidate structurally, because it uses the branch separation rather than ignoring it.

However, after filtering,

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm comm}
\]

is still a difference of two folded subset-product sums. Its phase families remain

\[
e^{-im\log(N_+/N_-)}
\]

and gamma-twisted variants. The filtered object has no visible positivity or diagonal dominance.

Thus the commutator is analytically feasible but not currently lower-bounded.

---

# 3. Effect of branch separation \(0<c<c_1<1/2\)

Branch separation gives:

\[
x^+\asymp c,
\qquad
x^-\asymp1-c,
\qquad
|x^+-x^-|\asymp1.
\]

This helps in three ways:

\[
\text{two-branch symbol rank},
\]

\[
\text{stable basis conditioning},
\]

\[
\text{construction of low-cost branch filters }L_\pm.
\]

But the incidence obstruction is phase-cancellation, not symbolic degeneracy. Branch separation does **not** rule out

\[
\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle\approx0.
\]

The reason is that the phase variables still enter through high-degree subset products. Even after branch filtering, the bad event is a folded subset-product cancellation conditioned on

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0.
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
0<c<c_1<1/2
\text{ is enough for rank and filtering, not enough for incidence.}
}
\]

---

# 4. The \(\alpha Q\) split

The branch weights contain opposite \(\alpha\)-biases:

\[
e^{-\alpha u},
\qquad
e^{+\alpha u^-},
\]

with

\[
u\sim cQ,
\qquad
u^-\sim(1-c)Q.
\]

## 4.1 Easier branch: \(|\alpha|Q\not\ll1\)

If

\[
|\alpha|Q\ge Q^{-\delta}
\]

or stronger,

\[
|\alpha|Q\gtrsim 1,
\]

then the two branch amplitudes may be strongly imbalanced:

\[
e^{-\alpha cQ}
\quad\text{versus}\quad
e^{+\alpha(1-c)Q}.
\]

In that regime, a branch-dominance theorem is plausible:

\[
|\alpha|Q\gtrsim1
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\max\{\|\mathbf z'_+\|,\|\mathbf z'_-\|\}
\ge
Q^C
\min\{\|\mathbf z'_+\|,\|\mathbf z'_-\|\}.
\]

If the dominant branch also has a minor-vector lower bound and is not internally self-cancelling, then an incidence lower bound may follow.

But this would prove only a separated-\(\alpha\) theorem. It does not cover the central hard case.

## 4.2 Hard branch: \(|\alpha|Q\ll1\)

When

\[
|\alpha|Q\ll1,
\]

the \(\alpha\)-bias disappears:

\[
e^{-\alpha u}=1+O(\alpha Q),
\]

\[
e^{+\alpha u^-}=1+O(\alpha Q).
\]

Then branch asymmetry is governed only by fixed algebraic/gamma factors such as

\[
x^{-3},
\]

saddle weights, and gamma phase derivatives.

Those are sufficient for symbol rank, but I do not see a sign, positivity, or phase obstruction.

Thus the uniform theorem still lives in the hard branch:

\[
\boxed{
|\alpha|Q\ll1.
}
\]

---

# 5. Search for sign, Hermitian positivity, or diagonal dominance

A successful invariant would ideally have a form like

\[
\mathcal I
=
\sum_\nu w_\nu |\mathcal A_\nu|^2
+
\text{small error},
\qquad
w_\nu\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

No such representation is visible.

The Wronskian is alternating/exterior. Its coefficients inherit:

\[
\text{basis determinant signs},
\]

\[
\text{AFE weight phases},
\]

\[
\text{gamma folding phases},
\]

\[
\text{subset-product oscillation}.
\]

The mixed terms

\[
e^{-im\log(N_+/N_-)}
\]

are especially hostile to positivity: different pairs \((N_+,N_-)\) can share nearby ratios, creating dense folded frequencies.

So neither the pure-main/pure-dual decomposition nor the mixed decomposition supplies diagonal dominance at fixed \(m_0\).

Average \(L^2\)-diagonal dominance is plausible, but pointwise incidence at \(Z_\alpha\)-zeros is not a consequence.

---

# 6. Concrete no-go model

Even with branch separation and healthy symbol rank, the following phase-relaxed model breaks all proposed invariants.

Let \(u_n^+\) and \(u_n^-\) be independent unit phases. Impose:

\[
Z_+(u^+)+Z_-(u^-)+Z_\Gamma=0,
\]

and

\[
\langle\mathbf z'(u^+,u^-),\mathbf M(u^+,u^-)\rangle=0.
\]

These are two complex equations. Since the packet-frequency block has many degrees of freedom, solutions are generically expected. The open conditions

\[
\rho_{\rm rank}\ge Q^{-C},
\qquad
\kappa_{\rm basis}\le Q^C,
\qquad
\|\mathbf z'\|,\|\mathbf M\|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

can be preserved.

Adding the conjugacy relation

\[
u_n^-=\overline{u_n^+}
\]

reduces freedom, but still leaves the one-parameter recurrence problem. Generic recurrence remains too cheap to rule out polynomial smallness.

Thus no invariant based only on branch separation, \(\alpha\)-bias, or formal main/dual folding can prove incidence.

---

# 7. Conditional theorem if an invariant is later found

The strongest useful statement is conditional:

## Conditional folded-invariant theorem

Assume ordinary packet scale, packet-frequency blocks with \(0<c<c_1<1/2\), and full canonical \(B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2\). Suppose there exists an invariant

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm fold}(m,\alpha)
\]

such that:

1. It is controlled by the Wronskian pairing:

\[
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge
Q^{-C}|\mathcal I_{\rm fold}(m,\alpha)|
-
Q^{-C'}.
\]

2. At \(Z_\alpha(m)=0\),

\[
|\mathcal I_{\rm fold}(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

Then

\[
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\Rightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Current status:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No concrete }\mathcal I_{\rm fold}\text{ satisfying condition 2 has been identified.}
}
\]

---

# 8. Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{No folded main/dual invariant currently closes incidence.}
}
\]

The deeper pass confirms:

\[
\text{branch separation}
\Rightarrow
\text{rank/filter feasibility},
\]

but not

\[
\text{pointwise non-cancellation}.
\]

The \(\alpha Q\not\ll1\) regime may admit a branch-imbalance theorem, but the uniform theorem still requires handling

\[
|\alpha|Q\ll1.
\]

Therefore the folded main/dual route still reduces to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual folded subset-product nonrecurrence conditioned on }Z_\alpha(m)=0.
}
\]

This lane remains Yellow. The ordinary packet-frequency Wronskian route is structurally coherent, but the incidence theorem remains a genuine actual-zeta arithmetic gap.
## User (2026-04-27 03:43:31)

Do the agent instruction in the message as per your agent number.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 03:43:38)

_Thought for 4s_

## Agent 4 / D — Folded main/dual invariant, final pass

Executing the assigned **Agent 4 / D** prompt: one final focused pass for a concrete folded main/dual invariant, with special attention to whether the \(|\alpha|Q\gtrsim1\) regime gives an easier theorem. The shared background says the Wronskian structure and stress tests are clean, and the remaining issue is actual-zeta incidence/nonrecurrence. 

---

# 1. Main/dual phase decomposition

At a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero,

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,
\]

the Wronskian is

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)
=
\langle\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle.
\]

Decompose the derivative row and cofactor vector:

\[
\mathbf z'
=
\mathbf z'_+
+
\mathbf z'_-
+
\mathbf z'_{\Gamma}
+
\mathbf z'_E,
\]

\[
\mathbf M
=
\mathbf M_+
+
\mathbf M_-
+
\mathbf M_{\rm mix}
+
\mathbf M_{\Gamma}
+
\mathbf M_E.
\]

Here \(+\) denotes the main AFE branch and \(-\) the folded dual branch.

The pairing splits into

\[
\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle
=
\langle\mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_+\rangle
+
\langle\mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_-\rangle
+
\langle\mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_-\rangle
+
\langle\mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_+\rangle
+
\langle\mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_{\rm mix}\rangle
+
\langle\mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_{\rm mix}\rangle
+
\cdots .
\]

The corresponding phase families are:

\[
e^{-im\log N_+},
\qquad
e^{+im\log N_-},
\]

\[
e^{-im\log(N_+/N_-)},
\]

and gamma-folded mixed phases of the form

\[
e^{ik\Theta(m)}e^{-im\log(N_+/N_-)}.
\]

The packet-frequency nodes are separated:

\[
x^+ = \lambda\log n\asymp c,
\qquad
x^-=\lambda\log\frac{m}{2\pi n}\asymp 1-c,
\]

with

\[
0<c<c_1<\frac12,
\]

so

\[
|x^+-x^-|\asymp1.
\]

This separation is real, but it is a symbol/filtering fact, not automatically a positivity fact.

---

# 2. Candidate invariant 1: skew invariant

Define

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm skew}
=
\operatorname{Im}
\langle\mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_-\rangle
-
\operatorname{Im}
\langle\mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_+\rangle.
\]

Leading phase expansion:

\[
\langle\mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_-\rangle
=
\sum_{n,S_-}
A_{n,S_-}^{+-}
e^{-im\log n}
e^{+im\log N_{S_-}}
+
\cdots
\]

\[
=
\sum_{n,S_-}
A_{n,S_-}^{+-}
e^{-im\log(n/N_{S_-})}
+
\cdots,
\]

and

\[
\langle\mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_+\rangle
=
\sum_{n,S_+}
A_{n,S_+}^{-+}
e^{+im\log n}
e^{-im\log N_{S_+}}
+
\cdots
\]

\[
=
\sum_{n,S_+}
A_{n,S_+}^{-+}
e^{-im\log(N_{S_+}/n)}
+
\cdots.
\]

So

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm skew}
\]

is a signed imaginary part of two mixed folded subset-product sums.

## Test for sign or dominance

No sign survives. At a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero, the branch equation is schematically

\[
Z_+ + Z_- \approx -Z_\Gamma.
\]

If \(Z_\Gamma\) is small or smooth, this allows

\[
Z_-\approx -Z_+.
\]

Then simple skew quantities like

\[
\operatorname{Im}(\overline{Z_+}Z_-)
\]

can vanish because

\[
\overline{Z_+}Z_-\approx -|Z_+|^2
\]

is real.

The same mechanism persists at the derivative/cofactor level: mixed terms can align so that the imaginary skew cancels. Branch separation changes the coefficient basis but does not fix the phase of the subset-product sum.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{\rm skew}
\text{ has no visible sign, positivity, or dominance.}
}
\]

---

# 3. Candidate invariant 2: commutator invariant

Let \(L_+\) and \(L_-\) be branch filters in \(\mathcal D\), with

\[
L_+\approx1\text{ on }x^+\asymp c,
\qquad
L_+\approx0\text{ on }x^-\asymp1-c,
\]

and conversely for \(L_-\).

Because

\[
|x^+-x^-|\asymp1,
\]

one can choose such filters with degree \(O(R)\) and cost

\[
\exp(O(R\log R))=Q^{O(1)}.
\]

Define

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm comm}
=
\langle L_+\mathbf z',L_-\mathbf M\rangle
-
\langle L_-\mathbf z',L_+\mathbf M\rangle.
\]

Leading expansion:

\[
\langle L_+\mathbf z',L_-\mathbf M\rangle
=
\sum_{n,S_-}
L_+(x_n^+)L_-(x_{S_-}^-)
A_{n,S_-}^{+-}
e^{-im\log(n/N_{S_-})}
+
\cdots,
\]

\[
\langle L_-\mathbf z',L_+\mathbf M\rangle
=
\sum_{n,S_+}
L_-(x_n^-)L_+(x_{S_+}^+)
A_{n,S_+}^{-+}
e^{-im\log(N_{S_+}/n)}
+
\cdots.
\]

The filters cleanly separate branches, so the commutator is a legitimate folded mixed-branch observable.

## Test for lower bound

The filtered commutator is still a difference of folded subset-product exponential sums. The phases

\[
\log(n/N_-),
\qquad
\log(N_+/n)
\]

are dense enough that pointwise cancellation remains possible.

A lower bound would need something like

\[
|\mathcal I_{\rm comm}(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

at \(Z_\alpha(m_0)=0\). But branch filtering alone gives only

\[
\text{symbol separation},
\]

not

\[
\text{phase noncancellation}.
\]

No Hermitian structure appears because the Wronskian cofactor vector is alternating, not a positive quadratic form.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal I_{\rm comm}
\text{ is the cleanest invariant structurally, but still reduces to folded subset-product nonrecurrence.}
}
\]

---

# 4. Candidate invariant 3: asymmetry invariant

Define

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm asym}
=
\langle\mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_+\rangle
-
\omega(m,\alpha)
\langle\mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_-\rangle,
\]

where \(\omega(m,\alpha)\) is the expected main/dual branch ratio from gamma folding and AFE weights.

Leading expansions:

\[
\langle\mathbf z'_+,\mathbf M_+\rangle
=
\sum_{n,S_+}
A_{n,S_+}^{++}
e^{-im\log(nN_{S_+})}
+
\cdots,
\]

\[
\langle\mathbf z'_-,\mathbf M_-\rangle
=
\sum_{n,S_-}
A_{n,S_-}^{--}
e^{+im\log(nN_{S_-})}
+
\cdots.
\]

So

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm asym}
\]

is a pure-branch imbalance.

## Test for dominance

The branch amplitudes contain opposite \(\alpha\)-biases:

\[
e^{-\alpha u},
\qquad
e^{+\alpha u^-},
\]

where

\[
u\sim cQ,
\qquad
u^-\sim(1-c)Q.
\]

Thus branch imbalance can occur when \(|\alpha|Q\) is not small.

---

# 5. Split by \(\alpha Q\)

## 5.1 Easier regime: \(|\alpha|Q\gtrsim1\)

If

\[
|\alpha|Q\gtrsim1,
\]

then

\[
e^{-\alpha cQ}
\]

and

\[
e^{+\alpha(1-c)Q}
\]

are separated by a factor roughly

\[
\exp(\alpha Q)
\]

up to \(c\)-dependent constants.

A conditional branch-dominance theorem is plausible:

### Candidate branch-dominance theorem

Assume ordinary scale, \(0<c<c_1<1/2\), and

\[
|\alpha|Q\ge C_0\log Q.
\]

Then one AFE branch dominates the other by a polynomial factor:

\[
\frac{\|\mathbf z'_+\|}{\|\mathbf z'_-\|}
\ge Q^{C}
\]

or

\[
\frac{\|\mathbf z'_-\|}{\|\mathbf z'_+\|}
\ge Q^{C}.
\]

If, additionally, the corresponding pure-branch minor vector satisfies

\[
\|\mathbf M_{\rm dom}\|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

and has no internal pure-branch cancellation, then

\[
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge Q^{-A'}.
\]

But this still needs a pure-branch minor nonrecurrence theorem. Branch dominance suppresses cross-branch cancellation but does not eliminate pure-branch subset-product cancellation.

So in this regime one can reduce the problem from folded main/dual incidence to **dominant-branch subset-product nonrecurrence**. This is easier, but not closed.

A more modest theorem is:

\[
|\alpha|Q\ge C_0\log Q
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{mixed main/dual cancellation is not the primary obstruction.}
\]

The remaining obstruction is then internal determinant cancellation inside the dominant branch.

## 5.2 Hard regime: \(|\alpha|Q\ll1\)

If

\[
|\alpha|Q\ll1,
\]

then

\[
e^{-\alpha u}=1+O(\alpha Q),
\]

\[
e^{+\alpha u^-}=1+O(\alpha Q).
\]

The \(\alpha\)-bias disappears. Branch asymmetry then comes only from:

\[
x^{-3}\text{-type symbol factors},
\]

gamma derivatives,

AFE weights,

and branch location \(c\) versus \(1-c\).

These distinguish rows enough for symbol rank, but I see no coercive sign, positivity, or determinant lower bound.

Thus the uniform theorem still hinges on the hard regime:

\[
\boxed{
|\alpha|Q\ll1.
}
\]

---

# 6. Positivity / Hermitian structure check

A successful invariant would ideally have

\[
\mathcal I
=
\sum_\nu w_\nu|\mathcal A_\nu|^2
+
\text{controlled error},
\qquad
w_\nu\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

No candidate above has this form.

The obstacle is intrinsic:

\[
\mathbf M
\]

is a cofactor vector from an alternating determinant. Its coefficients carry determinant signs and AFE phases. Mixed main/dual folded phases

\[
e^{-im\log(N_+/N_-)}
\]

do not pair naturally with their conjugates in a positive way.

So the pairing

\[
\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle
\]

is not a norm, energy, or Hermitian form. It is a determinant value.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No Hermitian positivity or diagonal dominance invariant is visible.}
}
\]

---

# 7. Final no-go for uniform folded invariant

For the uniform theorem, especially in the hard branch

\[
|\alpha|Q\ll1,
\]

all three candidate invariants reduce to pointwise noncancellation of folded subset-product sums.

Specifically:

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm skew}
\Rightarrow
\text{mixed folded subset-product nonrecurrence},
\]

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm comm}
\Rightarrow
\text{filtered mixed folded subset-product nonrecurrence},
\]

\[
\mathcal I_{\rm asym}
\Rightarrow
\text{pure branch subset-product nonrecurrence}.
\]

None supplies sign, positivity, or coercivity.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{folded main/dual arithmetic still reduces to subset-product nonrecurrence.}
}
\]

---

# 8. Final deliverable

## Concrete partial theorem

A limited \(\alpha\)-separated reduction is plausible:

\[
|\alpha|Q\ge C_0\log Q
\]

may imply polynomial branch dominance. In that regime, the folded incidence problem reduces to dominant-branch minor nonrecurrence rather than full mixed-branch angle cancellation.

But this does **not** close Gate 1, because:

1. the central regime \(|\alpha|Q\ll1\) remains;
2. dominant-branch subset-product nonrecurrence is still unproved.

## Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{No folded-invariant theorem presently closes incidence.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Branch separation and }\alpha\text{-asymmetry help rank and may simplify some regimes, but do not give a uniform lower bound.}
}
\]

The remaining uniform theorem is still:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m)=0
\Rightarrow
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A},
}
\]

equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual folded subset-product nonrecurrence conditioned on }Z_\alpha=0.
}
\]

## Assistant (2026-04-27 03:47:01)

_Thought for a couple of seconds_

## Agent 4 / D — Direct \(Z_\alpha\)-jet nonrecurrence from AFE coefficients

Executing the assigned Agent 4 task: bypass local amplitude / zero-crowding and attack directly

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\max_{1\le k\le R+1}
|\mathcal D^k Z_\alpha(m_0)|
\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The shared prompt narrows this round around polylog-local \(Z_\alpha\)-amplitude / zero-crowding, with Agent 4 assigned to the direct jet-nonrecurrence route. 

---

# 1. AFE jet vector form

Work in the ordinary packet-frequency regime:

\[
\lambda Q\asymp 1,
\qquad
R\asymp \frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\qquad
\mathcal D=\lambda\partial_m.
\]

Use main and folded-dual packet-frequency blocks. Write

\[
Z_\alpha(m)
=
Z_+(m,\alpha)+Z_-(m,\alpha)+Z_\Gamma(m,\alpha)+Z_E(m,\alpha),
\]

where schematically

\[
Z_+(m,\alpha)
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_+}
z_n^+(\alpha,m)e^{-im\log n},
\]

\[
Z_-(m,\alpha)
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_-}
z_n^-(\alpha,m)e^{+im\log n}.
\]

The normalized nodes are

\[
x_n^+=\lambda\log n,
\qquad
x_n^-=\lambda\log\frac{m}{2\pi n}
\]

up to the exact folded convention.

Then the \(Z_\alpha\)-jet vector is

\[
\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)
=
\left(
\mathcal D Z_\alpha,
\mathcal D^2 Z_\alpha,
\ldots,
\mathcal D^{R+1}Z_\alpha
\right)(m_0,\alpha).
\]

Ignoring slowly varying AFE amplitudes for the leading symbol, the main contribution is

\[
\mathbf z'_+
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_+}
z_n^+(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}
\mathbf v_+(x_n^+),
\]

with

\[
\mathbf v_+(x)
=
\left(
-ix,\,
(-ix)^2,\,
\ldots,\,
(-ix)^{R+1}
\right).
\]

Similarly the folded dual contribution is

\[
\mathbf z'_-
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_-}
z_n^-(\alpha,m_0)e^{+im_0\log n}
\mathbf v_-(x_n^-),
\]

with

\[
\mathbf v_-(y)
=
\left(
+iy,\,
(+iy)^2,\,
\ldots,\,
(+iy)^{R+1}
\right)
\]

plus lower-order corrections from gamma and saddle motion.

Thus

\[
\boxed{
\mathbf z'
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_+}
a_n^+u_n^+\mathbf v_+(x_n^+)
+
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_-}
a_n^-u_n^-\mathbf v_-(x_n^-)
+
\mathbf z'_\Gamma
+
\mathbf z'_E.
}
\]

Here

\[
u_n^+=e^{-im_0\log n},
\qquad
u_n^-=e^{+im_0\log n}.
\]

The zero condition itself is

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,
\]

i.e.

\[
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_+}a_n^+u_n^+
+
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_-}a_n^-u_n^-
+
Z_\Gamma+Z_E
=
0.
\]

So the direct bad event is

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,
\qquad
\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)\approx0.
}
\]

Equivalently, the value and first \(R+1\) normalized derivatives are simultaneously small.

---

# 2. Finite-dimensional interpretation

Define the augmented jet vector

\[
\mathcal J_{R+1}[Z_\alpha](m_0)
=
\left(
Z_\alpha,
\mathcal D Z_\alpha,
\ldots,
\mathcal D^{R+1}Z_\alpha
\right)(m_0).
\]

Then

\[
\mathcal J_{R+1}[Z_\alpha](m_0)
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_+}
a_n^+u_n^+
\mathbf w_+(x_n^+)
+
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_-}
a_n^-u_n^-
\mathbf w_-(x_n^-)
+
\text{background/error},
\]

where

\[
\mathbf w_+(x)=(1,-ix,\ldots,(-ix)^{R+1}),
\]

\[
\mathbf w_-(y)=(1,+iy,\ldots,(+iy)^{R+1}).
\]

The bad event is

\[
\mathcal J_{R+1}[Z_\alpha](m_0)\approx0.
\]

This is a finite moment-cancellation problem:

\[
\sum_n a_n^\pm u_n^\pm (x_n^\pm)^k
\approx 0,
\qquad
0\le k\le R+1.
\]

So direct jet nonrecurrence is a statement that the actual AFE phase vector cannot realize a high-order moment anti-design at an admissible \(Z_\alpha\)-zero.

---

# 3. Codimension in the relaxed phase model

In the phase-relaxed model, treat the phases

\[
u_n^+,\ u_n^-
\]

as independent unit complex variables.

The constraints are

\[
\mathcal J_{R+1}[Z_\alpha]\approx0,
\]

which is \(R+2\) complex equations, hence \(2R+4\) real constraints.

The number of phase variables in a packet-frequency block is much larger than \(R\):

\[
|\mathcal B_{\rm pf}|\gg R.
\]

Therefore, in the relaxed torus, the system is not overdetermined. With enough active frequencies, one can solve

\[
\sum_n a_nu_n x_n^k=0,
\qquad 0\le k\le R+1,
\]

by a finite quadrature / polygonal balancing construction, provided coefficient magnitudes are sufficiently distributed.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Direct }Z_\alpha\text{-jet nonrecurrence is false in the phase-relaxed model.}
}
\]

It must use the actual one-parameter orbit

\[
u_n(m)=e^{-im\log n},
\]

not just symbol rank or moment count.

---

# 4. One-parameter recurrence cost

On the actual orbit, the bad event is

\[
\left|
\mathcal J_{R+1}[Z_\alpha](m)
\right|
\le Q^{-A}.
\]

This is roughly a tube around the origin in \(\mathbb C^{R+2}\). A naive independent-value model gives small-ball probability

\[
Q^{-2A(R+2)}.
\]

Thus recurrence time would be roughly

\[
T_{\rm rec}
\sim
Q^{2A(R+2)}.
\]

At

\[
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q},
\]

this is

\[
T_{\rm rec}
=
\exp\left(
O\left(\frac{(\log Q)^2}{\log\log Q}\right)
\right).
\]

This is much larger than the codimension-four incidence recurrence, but still far below a full height range if \(Q\sim\log m\) and \(m\) can range up to \(e^Q\).

So recurrence cost alone does not obviously prove the theorem globally.

However, this is a sharper and possibly more useful obstruction than the Wronskian-pair incidence. It says a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero with flat jet through order \(R+1\) requires approximate recurrence into a codimension-\(2R+4\) tube.

A possible theorem target is therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual }Z_\alpha\text{ prime-log orbit avoids the jet-flat tube at }Z_\alpha\text{-zeros.}
}
\]

But this remains a genuine arithmetic nonrecurrence theorem.

---

# 5. Does AFE structure forbid high-order flat zeros?

For an ordinary analytic function, a simple zero can be arbitrarily flat in the sense that

\[
F(0)=0,
\qquad
F'(0)\ne0
\]

but \(|F'(0)|\) may be extremely small.

To forbid this for \(Z_\alpha\), one needs additional structure.

Possible sources:

## 5.1 Exponential-polynomial complexity

A finite exponential polynomial

\[
F(t)=\sum_n b_ne^{-itx_n}
\]

with frequencies in a bounded interval and nontrivial coefficient mass cannot have too many zeros or too high a flatness unless coefficients satisfy a moment anti-design.

But in a packet-frequency block,

\[
x_n\in[c,c+1/R],
\]

or after normalization,

\[
\operatorname{diam}(x_n)\asymp R^{-1}.
\]

The frequencies are clustered. Such clustering allows high-order moment cancellation by choosing many coefficients/phases. Therefore finite exponential-polynomial complexity alone is not enough.

## 5.2 Exact AFE coefficients

The coefficients are not arbitrary:

\[
b_n=z_n^\pm(\alpha,m)e^{\mp im\log n}.
\]

The magnitudes and phases come from actual \(m\), not free choices. This is the only real source of rigidity.

So the theorem must exploit the fixed orbit

\[
m\mapsto(e^{-im\log n})_n.
\]

## 5.3 Main/dual coupling

The main/dual system doubles the branch constraints. It may help if the same \(m\) must make both branches flat in compatible but distinct packet-frequency coordinates:

\[
x^+\asymp c,
\qquad
x^-\asymp1-c.
\]

A joint flatness condition is then

\[
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_+}a_n^+u_n^+(x_n^+)^k
+
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B_-}a_n^-u_n^-(x_n^-)^k
\approx0
\]

for

\[
0\le k\le R+1.
\]

Branch separation may raise the effective codimension, but it still does not provide a sign or diagonal obstruction.

Thus main/dual coupling improves plausibility but does not by itself prove nonflatness.

---

# 6. Equivalence to local amplitude / zero-crowding

The direct jet theorem and local amplitude theorem are essentially equivalent under analytic upper control.

If

\[
\max_{1\le k\le R+1}\frac{|F^{(k)}(0)|}{k!}
\le Q^{-A}
\]

and

\[
\sup_{|t|\le A}|F(t)|\le Q^{A_1},
\]

then Taylor expansion plus Cauchy tail estimates imply

\[
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F(t)|
\le Q^{-A'} + \text{tail}.
\]

Choosing

\[
A/a\ge(\log Q)^C
\]

and

\[
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}
\]

makes the tail polynomially small. Hence jet-flatness implies local amplitude smallness.

Conversely, if local amplitude is polynomially large, then some derivative through \(R+1\) must be polynomially large.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{direct }Z_\alpha\text{-jet nonrecurrence}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\text{polylog-local }Z_\alpha\text{-amplitude}
}
\]

modulo analytic upper bounds.

Thus this route does not avoid the local amplitude / zero-crowding problem; it reformulates it in moment-vector language.

---

# 7. Candidate direct jet theorem

A viable theorem statement is:

## Direct \(Z_\alpha\)-jet nonrecurrence theorem

Let

\[
F(t)=Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t),
\qquad
F(0)=0,
\qquad
\lambda Q\asymp1.
\]

Assume \(m_0,\alpha\) are admissible and \(Z_\alpha\) is represented by the full canonical packet-frequency AFE with controlled error through order \(R+1\). Then

\[
\max_{1\le k\le R+1}
\frac{|F^{(k)}(0)|}{k!}
\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Equivalently,

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

A sufficient arithmetic hypothesis is:

\[
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
(e^{-im_0\log n})_{n\in\mathcal B_{\rm pf}},
\mathcal F_{\rm jet-flat}
\right)
\ge Q^{-A},
\]

where \(\mathcal F_{\rm jet-flat}\) is the finite-dimensional moment-flat variety

\[
\left\{
u:
\sum_n a_n^\pm u_n^\pm(x_n^\pm)^k=0,\quad 0\le k\le R+1
\right\}.
\]

This is precise but still an actual-orbit nonrecurrence theorem.

---

# 8. No-go diagnosis

The direct jet route does not currently close the gap.

It improves the shape of the missing theorem:

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,\quad
\mathbf z'(m_0)\approx0
\]

is a higher-codimension event than

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0,\quad
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0)\approx0.
\]

But:

1. It is false in the phase-relaxed model.
2. Generic one-parameter recurrence may still be too cheap globally.
3. Main/dual branch separation improves rank but gives no sign.
4. Under analytic upper control, it is equivalent to local amplitude / zero-crowding.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{direct }Z_\alpha\text{-jet nonrecurrence is not an independent route.}
}
\]

It is a clean reformulation of the polylog-local \(Z_\alpha\)-amplitude theorem.

---

# 9. Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{No direct AFE-coefficient proof of }|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}\text{ yet.}
}
\]

The best theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
(e^{-im_0\log n})_n,
\mathcal F_{\rm jet-flat}
\right)
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

But this is exactly new actual-zeta prime-log nonrecurrence.

So Agent 4’s conclusion:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Direct jet nonrecurrence is equivalent, modulo analytic bounds, to local }Z_\alpha\text{-amplitude / zero-crowding.}
}
\]

The route remains Yellow.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 04:11:17)

_Thought for 12s_

## Agent 4 / D — Direct incidence via actual-zeta small-value repulsion

Executing the assigned Agent 4 task: attack

\[
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}
\]

as an actual-orbit avoidance problem. The current prompt asks for either a direct small-value repulsion theorem candidate or a precise actual-orbit nonrecurrence theorem sufficient for the Wronskian route. 

---

# 1. Actual-orbit formulation

Let the packet-frequency block be

\[
\mathcal B_{\rm pf}
=
\{n:cQ\le \log n\le cQ+Q/R\},
\qquad 0<c<1/2,
\]

and define phase coordinates

\[
u_n(m)=e^{-im\log n}.
\]

For the folded dual branch, write either

\[
u_n^-(m)=e^{+im\log n}=\overline{u_n(m)}
\]

or, with gamma folding, absorb the extra smooth phase into the coefficient:

\[
u_n^-(m)=e^{i\Theta(m)}e^{+im\log n}.
\]

Then the actual orbit is the one-dimensional curve

\[
u(m)
=
\bigl(u_n^+(m),u_n^-(m)\bigr)_{n\in\mathcal B_{\rm pf}}
\]

inside a finite-dimensional torus.

The bad set is

\[
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}(\alpha)
=
\left\{
u:
Z_\alpha(u)=0,\ 
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(u,\alpha)=0
\right\}.
\]

A direct incidence theorem is exactly an avoidance estimate:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}\bigl(u(m),\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}(\alpha)\bigr)
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for admissible \(m,\alpha\), in a metric where the coordinate weights match the AFE coefficients and the basis-normalized Wronskian conditioning.

More concretely, since

\[
Z_\alpha
\]

and

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R
\]

are locally Lipschitz in the phase variables with polynomially controlled derivatives after basis normalization, a distance lower bound

\[
\operatorname{dist}(u(m),\mathcal Z_{\rm bad})\ge Q^{-A_0}
\]

would imply

\[
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A_1}.
\]

So the direct theorem can be stated as an actual-orbit nonrecurrence theorem.

---

# 2. Structure of \(\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}\)

On the finite phase torus, the equations have the schematic form

\[
Z_\alpha(u)
=
\sum_n a_n^+u_n^+
+
\sum_n a_n^-u_n^-
+
Z_\Gamma
+
Z_E,
\]

and

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(u,\alpha)
=
\sum_\nu B_\nu(\alpha)U_\nu
+
W_\Gamma
+
W_E,
\]

where the monomials \(U_\nu\) include pure-main, pure-dual, and mixed folded subset products:

\[
U_\nu
\in
\left\{
\prod_{n\in S_+}u_n^+,\ 
\prod_{n\in S_-}u_n^-,\ 
\prod_{n\in S_+}u_n^+
\prod_{n\in S_-}u_n^-
\right\}.
\]

Thus \(Z_\alpha\) is essentially linear in the phase variables, while \(\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\) is a high-degree Laurent polynomial.

The phase-space bad variety is therefore

\[
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}
=
\{Z_\alpha=0\}\cap\{\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R=0\}.
\]

In the relaxed complex torus, this variety is not expected to be empty. There are many phase variables and only two complex equations. Unless \(\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\) restricts to a nonzero constant on the hyperplane \(Z_\alpha=0\), which it does not, the intersection should generically have positive dimension.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Exact AFE coefficients do not appear to make }\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}\text{ empty as a phase-space variety.}
}
\]

The theorem must use the actual one-parameter orbit

\[
m\mapsto u(m),
\]

not merely algebraic nonexistence in phase space.

---

# 3. Does exact AFE structure lower the dimension of \(\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}\)?

Only partially.

The exact AFE structure gives:

\[
\text{canonical coefficients,}
\]

\[
\text{main/dual coupling,}
\]

\[
\text{gamma folding,}
\]

\[
\text{basis-normalized determinant coefficients,}
\]

and

\[
\text{controlled }B_2,E_2.
\]

These remove fake toy degeneracies. They also make symbol rank healthy on packet-frequency blocks.

But they do not visibly impose an algebraic incompatibility of the form

\[
Z_\alpha(u)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(u,\alpha)\ne0
\]

on the whole torus.

The best one can currently say is:

\[
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}
\]

is likely a genuine codimension-two complex variety inside the relaxed phase torus, not an empty set. The actual theorem must show that the zeta orbit avoids this variety at polynomial scale.

---

# 4. Candidate actual-orbit repulsion theorem

The clean sufficient theorem is:

## Actual-orbit incidence repulsion theorem

Fix ordinary scale

\[
\lambda Q\asymp1,
\qquad
R\asymp\frac{\log Q}{\log\log Q}.
\]

Let \(\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\) be the full canonical basis-normalized Wronskian built from

\[
q''_{\can}=B_2+P_2+D_2+E_2.
\]

Assume packet-frequency symbol rank and canonical AFE error control. Then for every admissible \(m,\alpha\),

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}_{\rm pf}
\bigl(
u(m),
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}(\alpha)
\bigr)
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A'}.
}
\]

At a zero \(Z_\alpha(m_0)=0\), this gives

\[
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A'}.
\]

This theorem would close the incidence step.

But this is a new actual-zeta arithmetic theorem. It cannot be derived from determinant algebra alone.

---

# 5. What actual-zeta feature could prove avoidance?

## 5.1 Euler-product rigidity

A possible mechanism is that the orbit

\[
m\mapsto(e^{-im\log p})_p
\]

is not merely a generic Kronecker orbit, because the same primes also determine the AFE coefficients, dual folding, and the zeta zero condition.

The needed theorem would say:

\[
Z_\alpha(m)\approx0
\]

already consumes the available recurrence freedom, so the orbit cannot also satisfy

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)\approx0.
\]

However, generic recurrence heuristics do not support this automatically. The orbit can approximate finite-codimension conditions at polynomial scale unless actual coefficients impose special rigidity.

So Euler-product rigidity is the right conceptual input, but no concrete inequality is presently identified.

## 5.2 Main/dual coupling

The folded dual relation links the main and dual phases:

\[
u_n^-=\overline{u_n^+}
\]

up to gamma folding.

This cuts the phase freedom relative to the fully relaxed model. It also creates separated packet-frequency branches

\[
x^+\asymp c,
\qquad
x^-\asymp1-c.
\]

But prior analysis found no sign, positivity, Hermitian form, or commutator invariant. So main/dual coupling improves the fidelity of the bad variety but does not currently prove avoidance.

## 5.3 Coefficient sign pattern

A sign-pattern proof would require

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R
\]

or a projection of it to have a sign-definite expansion on \(Z_\alpha=0\).

But the determinant coefficients have alternating signs, AFE phases, gamma phases, and subset-product oscillations. No sign-definite sector is visible.

## 5.4 Zero-repulsion

At \(Z_\alpha(m_0)=0\),

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m_0,\alpha)
=
\langle\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha),\mathbf M(m_0)\rangle.
\]

A zero-repulsion theorem would assert that the \(Z_\alpha\)-derivative vector cannot align orthogonally to the cofactor vector:

\[
|\langle\mathbf z',\mathbf M\rangle|
\ge
Q^{-A}\|\mathbf z'\|\,\|\mathbf M\|.
\]

This is exactly the cofactor-form incidence theorem. It is useful as a formulation, not a proof.

## 5.5 Explicit-formula imbalance

A proof might show that

\[
Z_\alpha\approx0
\]

and

\[
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\approx0
\]

force incompatible prime/zero statistics under the explicit formula.

The obstruction is that \(Z_\alpha\) is linear, while \(\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\) is exterior/nonlinear. The explicit formula handles linear statistics cleanly; the determinant becomes a folded subset-product condition. So this route again becomes actual folded subset-product nonrecurrence.

---

# 6. Why generic recurrence is insufficient

The common-small event is

\[
|Z_\alpha(m)|\le \varepsilon,
\qquad
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|\le \varepsilon.
\]

For two complex observables, a generic model gives codimension four and recurrence probability roughly

\[
\varepsilon^4.
\]

At

\[
\varepsilon=Q^{-A},
\]

the recurrence time is

\[
Q^{4A},
\]

which is too cheap over long height ranges when \(Q\sim\log m\).

Therefore a polynomial lower bound cannot come from codimension alone. It must use a special arithmetic exclusion.

---

# 7. Strongest sufficient nonrecurrence theorem

The most precise sufficient statement is:

## Packet-frequency actual-orbit avoidance theorem

Let

\[
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}^{(\varepsilon)}
=
\left\{
u:
|Z_\alpha(u)|\le \varepsilon,\ 
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(u,\alpha)|\le \varepsilon
\right\}.
\]

Then for some \(A>0\),

\[
u(m)\notin
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}^{(Q^{-A})}
\]

for all admissible \(m,\alpha\).

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\inf_{m,\alpha\ {\rm admissible}}
\left(
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\right)
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is the exact black-box theorem needed by the direct incidence route.

A slightly weaker zero-conditioned version suffices:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\operatorname{dist}_{\rm pf}
\bigl(u(m_0),\{u:\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(u,\alpha)=0\}\bigr)
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

---

# 8. Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{No direct small-value repulsion theorem is presently proved.}
}
\]

The exact AFE coefficients and main/dual structure do not appear to make the relaxed bad variety empty. They define a more canonical bad set, but the actual proof still needs an avoidance theorem for the zeta prime-log orbit.

So the best deliverable is the precise sufficient theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}
\bigl(
u(m),
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}(\alpha)
\bigr)
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

or equivalently

\[
\boxed{
|Z_\alpha(m)|+
|\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)|
\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is not yet proved. It is the remaining actual-zeta arithmetic gap for the ordinary packet-frequency Wronskian route.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 04:16:43)

_Thought for 12s_

## Agent 4 / D — Direct jet-flat variety vs. cell-sum variety

Executing the assigned Agent 4 task: compare the direct jet-flat bad event with the coarse cell-sum bad event for the polylog-local \(Z_\alpha\)-amplitude route. The current prompt identifies the cell-sum theorem as the best concrete subtarget for proving local amplitude and hence \(|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}\). 

---

# 1. Common setup

Write the packet-frequency AFE model as

\[
F(t)=Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t)
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B} b_n e^{-itx_n}
+
\mathrm{dual/background/error},
\]

where

\[
b_n=z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n},
\qquad
x_n=\lambda\log n.
\]

For the comparison, first ignore dual/background/error and study the model

\[
F(t)=\sum_n b_n e^{-itx_n}.
\]

The direct jet-flat event is

\[
F(0)\approx0,
\qquad
F^{(k)}(0)\approx0,
\quad 1\le k\le R+1.
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\mu_k:=\sum_n b_n x_n^k\approx0,
\qquad
0\le k\le R+1.
\]

The cell-sum event is

\[
B_r:=\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_n\approx0
\quad\text{for all cells }I_r.
\]

So the comparison is between:

\[
\boxed{
\text{low-degree moment cancellation}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
\text{localized frequency-cell cancellation}.
}
\]

---

# 2. Cell sums imply jet-flatness only with the right cell scale

Assume the \(x\)-support is partitioned into cells

\[
I_r=[\xi_r-\delta/2,\xi_r+\delta/2],
\]

with

\[
|I_r|=\delta.
\]

Then

\[
\mu_k
=
\sum_r\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_n x_n^k.
\]

Write

\[
x_n^k
=
\xi_r^k
+
O(k\delta\,X^{k-1})
\]

inside \(I_r\), where \(X\asymp1\) on ordinary packet-frequency blocks. Therefore

\[
\mu_k
=
\sum_r \xi_r^k B_r
+
O\left(
k\delta
\sum_n |b_n|
\right).
\]

Thus, if

\[
B_r\approx0
\quad\text{for all }r
\]

and

\[
\delta\ll \frac{Q^{-A}}{R\sum_n|b_n|},
\]

then

\[
\mu_k\approx0
\qquad
0\le k\le R+1.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum smallness at sufficiently fine scale implies jet-flatness.}
}
\]

But the needed fineness may be too strong. If the cell width is only

\[
\delta\asymp \frac1a,
\]

with

\[
a\asymp R,
\]

then the Taylor error is approximately

\[
O\left(\frac{k}{R}\sum_n|b_n|\right).
\]

For \(k\le R\), this is not automatically small. It is only controlled if the cell-level first moments are also controlled:

\[
B_r^{(1)}:=\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_n(x_n-\xi_r),
\]

and similarly for higher intra-cell moments.

Therefore the correct implication is:

## Proposition 1 — cell hierarchy implies jet-flatness

If for each cell \(I_r\),

\[
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_n(x_n-\xi_r)^j
\approx0,
\qquad
0\le j\le J,
\]

and the residual Taylor tail for \(j>J\) is \(Q^{-A}\)-small for \(k\le R+1\), then

\[
\mu_k\approx0,
\qquad
0\le k\le R+1.
\]

Plain zeroth-order cell sums \(B_r\) alone imply jet-flatness only when the cell scale is fine enough or the intra-cell moments are controlled.

---

# 3. Jet-flatness does not imply cell-sum smallness

The converse is false in general.

Suppose the support has \(M\) cells and define the coarse cell vector

\[
B=(B_1,\ldots,B_M).
\]

The low moments approximately satisfy

\[
\mu_k
\approx
\sum_{r=1}^{M}B_r\xi_r^k,
\qquad
0\le k\le R+1.
\]

In matrix form,

\[
\mu=V B,
\]

where

\[
V_{k r}=\xi_r^k.
\]

If

\[
M>R+2,
\]

then \(V\) has a nontrivial kernel. Hence there are nonzero cell vectors \(B\ne0\) such that

\[
\sum_r B_r\xi_r^k=0,
\qquad
0\le k\le R+1.
\]

Therefore one can have

\[
\mu_k=0
\quad
0\le k\le R+1
\]

while

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2
\]

is large.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{jet-flatness does not imply cell-sum smallness if the number of cells exceeds }R+2.
}
\]

This is the standard finite-moment nullspace obstruction.

If the number of cells satisfies

\[
M\le R+2
\]

and the nodes \(\xi_r\) are well separated, then \(V\) is injective and one obtains

\[
\|B\|_2
\le
\|V^{-1}\|\,\|\mu\|_2.
\]

But \(\|V^{-1}\|\) may be exponentially large unless the basis is normalized and the cell centers are well conditioned.

Thus even in the injective regime, one needs basis conditioning.

---

# 4. Which event is stronger?

The answer depends on the number of cells.

## Case A: few cells, \(M\lesssim R\)

If the cell partition has

\[
M\le R+2
\]

and a stable basis is used, then moment flatness and cell-sum flatness are essentially equivalent up to conditioning:

\[
\|\mu\|_2\asymp_{\mathrm{cond}}\|B\|_2.
\]

In this regime,

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}
\]

is roughly equivalent to

\[
\max_{0\le k\le R+1}|\mu_k|\ge Q^{-A'}.
\]

So the cell-sum theorem is not stronger; it is a better-conditioned coordinate version of jet nonflatness.

## Case B: many cells, \(M\gg R\)

If

\[
M\gg R,
\]

then cell-sum non-cancellation is strictly stronger than jet nonflatness.

Indeed, low moments only see the projection of \(B\) onto the degree-\(R\) polynomial subspace. A large cell vector \(B\) can hide in the orthogonal complement.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum non-cancellation is stronger than jet nonflatness when the partition is too fine.}
}
\]

## Case C: single narrow determinant block

For one ordinary determinant block

\[
cQ\le \log n\le cQ+Q/R,
\]

we have

\[
\operatorname{diam}(x_n)\asymp R^{-1}.
\]

If the local window is

\[
a\asymp R,
\]

then cell width is

\[
\delta\asymp a^{-1}\asymp R^{-1}.
\]

Thus the entire determinant block may occupy only \(O(1)\) cells. In that case, the zeroth cell sum is essentially the same as

\[
F(0)=\sum_n b_n,
\]

which is already zero or small at a \(Z_\alpha\)-zero. Therefore, a single determinant block is not enough for a meaningful cell-sum theorem.

The cell-sum method must use either:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a wider packet-frequency support made of many }Q/R\text{-blocks,}
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{multiple separated packet-frequency blocks.}
}
\]

This is important. Otherwise the cell-sum diagnostic degenerates into the already-cancelled \(Z_\alpha(m_0)=0\) condition.

---

# 5. Relaxed phase model comparisons

## 5.1 Jet-flat but cell sums large

Choose \(M>R+2\) cells and a nonzero vector

\[
B\in\ker V,
\qquad
V_{kr}=\xi_r^k,
\quad 0\le k\le R+1.
\]

Then

\[
\mu_k=0
\quad
0\le k\le R+1,
\]

but

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2>0.
\]

Realize each \(B_r\) by choosing phases \(b_n\) inside cell \(I_r\). This gives a phase-relaxed model with jet-flatness but large cell mass.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{jet-flatness does not force cell-sum collapse.}
}
\]

## 5.2 Cell sums small but jets live

If cell sums vanish only at zeroth order,

\[
B_r=0,
\]

but first intra-cell moments

\[
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_n(x_n-\xi_r)
\]

are large, then the first and higher moments may remain live:

\[
\mu_1,\mu_2,\ldots \not\approx0.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{zeroth cell-sum smallness does not force jet-flatness unless cells are fine or intra-cell moments are controlled.}
}
\]

## 5.3 Both bad events can be realized in a relaxed model

With enough active phases, one can impose both:

\[
\mu_k=0,
\qquad
0\le k\le R+1,
\]

and

\[
B_r=0
\quad\text{for all }r
\]

provided the number of phase variables exceeds the number of constraints. Thus the relaxed model does not rule out either bad event.

The actual one-parameter prime-log orbit is again the only possible source of rigidity.

---

# 6. Which formulation is more promising?

The answer is: **cell sums are more promising as a diagnostic and \(L^2\)-amplitude route, but not as a strictly equivalent theorem.**

Reasons:

1. Cell sums align with the \(L^2\) amplitude calculation on windows \(a\asymp R\) or polylog-sized windows.

2. Cell sums produce a positive quantity:

   \[
   \sum_r|B_r|^2,
   \]

   whereas low moments are oscillatory.

3. Cell sums are numerically stable and diagnose whether local amplitude is hidden by coherent packet cancellation.

4. But the theorem

   \[
   \sum_r|B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}
   \]

   is stronger than necessary if the number of cells is large.

5. The direct jet theorem is exactly what is needed for \(|\mathbf z'|\), but it is less positive and harder to attack with \(L^2\) methods.

Thus the recommended route is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{use cell sums to prove amplitude, not to characterize jet-flatness exactly.}
}
\]

---

# 7. Theorem-ready comparison

## Theorem A — Cell mass implies local amplitude

Let

\[
F(t)=\sum_n b_ne^{-itx_n}.
\]

Let \(I_r\) be a partition of the active frequency support into cells of width

\[
\delta\asymp a^{-1}.
\]

Let

\[
B_r=\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_n.
\]

Assume the cell centers are separated at scale \(\delta\), and the cell approximation error is controlled. Then

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2dt
\ge
c a\sum_r|B_r|^2
-
\mathrm{Err}_{\rm intra}.
\]

Therefore, if

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}
\]

and

\[
\mathrm{Err}_{\rm intra}\le \frac12 caQ^{-A},
\]

then

\[
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A'}.
\]

Combined with the amplitude-to-derivative theorem, this gives

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A''}.
\]

## Theorem B — Jet moments and cell sums are conditionally equivalent

Let \(M\le R+2\), and let the cell centers \(\xi_r\) be well conditioned in a degree-\(R+1\) basis. Then

\[
\max_{0\le k\le R+1}|\mu_k|
\ge Q^{-A}
\]

if and only if

\[
\|B\|_2\ge Q^{-A'}
\]

up to conditioning losses.

If \(M>R+2\), the implication from jet nonflatness to cell mass fails in general, and the implication from cell mass to jet nonflatness also fails unless one projects onto the low-degree polynomial subspace.

---

# 8. Final recommendation

Prioritize the cell-sum route for **local amplitude**, not as an equivalent replacement for the direct jet theorem.

The best theorem to attack is:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_r
\left|
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}
\right|^2
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for a partition of a sufficiently wide packet-frequency support, not just one \(Q/R\)-block.

This theorem is stronger than necessary but has two advantages:

\[
\boxed{
\text{it is positive,}
\qquad
\text{and it directly implies polylog-local amplitude.}
}
\]

However, it remains an actual prime-log non-cancellation theorem. It is false in relaxed models and cannot be proved by moment algebra alone.

Final verdict:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum non-cancellation is a useful sufficient route to }|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A},
}
\]

but

\[
\boxed{
\text{it is not equivalent to direct jet nonflatness except in carefully conditioned few-cell regimes.}
}
\]

## Assistant (2026-04-27 05:43:48)

## Agent 4 / D — Jet-flat variety vs. multi-block cell-sum variety

Executing Agent 4’s assigned task: compare direct jet-flatness and cell-sum cancellation in the **multi-block** setting, and decide whether the next proof target should be jets, raw cell sums, or a projected low-degree cell component. The prompt explicitly warns that a single \(Q/R\)-width block can make cell sums degenerate into the already-cancelled total value \(F(0)=0\), so the comparison must use wider support or multiple blocks. 

---

# 1. Common model

Work with

\[
F(t)=Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t)
=
\sum_{n\in\mathcal B} b_n e^{-itx_n}
+
\text{dual/background/error},
\]

where

\[
b_n=z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n},
\qquad
x_n=\lambda\log n.
\]

Use a multi-block packet-frequency support

\[
\mathcal B_{\rm wide}
=
\{n:cQ\le \log n\le cQ+L_{\rm wide}\},
\]

where

\[
L_{\rm wide}=K\frac QR
\]

for some integer or slowly growing parameter \(K\). In normalized \(x\)-coordinates,

\[
\operatorname{diam}(x)
=
\lambda L_{\rm wide}
\asymp
\frac{K}{R}.
\]

Partition the \(x\)-support into cells

\[
I_r,\qquad |I_r|\asymp R^{-1}.
\]

Then the number of cells is

\[
M\asymp K.
\]

Define

\[
B_r=\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_n.
\]

The direct jet moments are

\[
\mu_k
=
\frac{i^k}{k!}F^{(k)}(0)
=
\sum_n b_n x_n^k,
\qquad
0\le k\le R+1.
\]

Ignoring intra-cell variation first, write

\[
\mu_k
\approx
\sum_{r=1}^M B_r \xi_r^k,
\]

where \(\xi_r\) is a cell center.

So the comparison reduces to a finite-dimensional map

\[
B=(B_1,\ldots,B_M)
\longmapsto
\mu=(\mu_0,\ldots,\mu_{R+1})
\]

through a Vandermonde-like moment matrix

\[
V_{kr}=\xi_r^k.
\]

---

# 2. Cell sums \(\Rightarrow\) local amplitude

The cell-sum route is not primarily a jet-equivalence route. It is an \(L^2\)-amplitude route.

Let

\[
F_{\rm cell}(t)=\sum_r B_r e^{-it\xi_r}.
\]

For

\[
a\asymp R,
\]

cell separation

\[
|\xi_r-\xi_s|\asymp R^{-1}
\]

is resolvable by the kernel

\[
K_a(\xi)=\int_{-a}^{a}e^{-it\xi}\,dt.
\]

Thus

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F_{\rm cell}(t)|^2dt
=
\sum_{r,s}B_r\overline{B_s}K_a(\xi_r-\xi_s).
\]

If the cells are reasonably separated and the window is chosen with mild smoothing, the Gram matrix

\[
(K_a(\xi_r-\xi_s))_{r,s}
\]

has a lower frame bound of size

\[
\gg a Q^{-O(1)}
\]

provided \(M\lesssim R^{O(1)}\) and the cell centers are not pathologically clustered.

Therefore,

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2dt
\ge
Q^{-O(1)}a\sum_r|B_r|^2
-
\mathrm{Err}_{\rm intra}
-
\mathrm{Err}_{\rm dual/bg/E}.
\]

Hence

\[
\sum_r |B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}
\]

implies

\[
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F(t)|
\ge Q^{-A'}
\]

and then the already-established amplitude-to-derivative theorem gives

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A''}.
\]

So the route

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell mass}
\Rightarrow
\text{polylog-local amplitude}
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

is valid and useful.

---

# 3. Jet-flatness versus cell-sum cancellation

## 3.1 Jet-flat event

The direct jet-flat event is

\[
\mu_k\approx0,
\qquad
0\le k\le R+1.
\]

In finite-dimensional terms,

\[
VB\approx0.
\]

## 3.2 Cell-sum bad event

The raw cell-sum bad event is

\[
B_r\approx0
\qquad
\forall r.
\]

This is much stronger than

\[
VB\approx0
\]

when \(M\) is large.

---

# 4. Regime \(M\le R+2\)

If

\[
M\le R+2,
\]

and the cell centers \(\xi_r\) are well-conditioned in a stable basis, then

\[
B\mapsto \mu
\]

is injective.

Using a basis-normalized matrix \(\Phi_{kr}=\phi_k(\xi_r)\), \(0\le k\le R+1\), we have

\[
\|\mu\|_2\asymp_{\kappa(\Phi)}\|B\|_2.
\]

If

\[
\kappa(\Phi)\le Q^C,
\]

then

\[
\|B\|_2\le Q^C\|\mu\|_2
\]

and

\[
\|\mu\|_2\le Q^C\|B\|_2.
\]

Thus in the few-cell regime,

\[
\boxed{
\text{jet nonflatness and cell-mass noncancellation are polynomially equivalent.}
}
\]

This is the cleanest setting for a proof. The support should have enough cells not to collapse to \(F(0)\), but not so many that the low-degree moments lose control.

Recommended range:

\[
\boxed{
2\le M\le c_0R
}
\]

with stable basis normalization. In practice, something like

\[
M\asymp R/4,\quad R/2,\quad R
\]

is mathematically more meaningful than \(M\gg R\).

---

# 5. Regime \(M\gg R\)

If

\[
M>R+2,
\]

then \(V\) has a nontrivial kernel.

There exist nonzero cell vectors

\[
B\ne0
\]

such that

\[
\sum_{r=1}^M B_r\xi_r^k=0,
\qquad
0\le k\le R+1.
\]

So one can have

\[
\mu_k=0
\quad
0\le k\le R+1
\]

but

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2>0.
\]

Therefore,

\[
\boxed{
\text{jet-flatness does not imply cell-sum cancellation when }M\gg R.
}
\]

Conversely, if all raw cell sums vanish,

\[
B_r=0,
\]

then the coarse moments vanish, but the true moments may still receive intra-cell contributions

\[
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_n(x_n-\xi_r)^j,
\qquad j\ge1.
\]

So raw zeroth-order cell cancellation does not imply jet-flatness unless cells are sufficiently narrow or one also controls higher intra-cell moments.

Thus in \(M\gg R\),

\[
\boxed{
\text{raw cell sums and jets are not equivalent.}
}
\]

The correct object is not the full cell vector \(B\), but its projection onto low-degree polynomial modes.

---

# 6. Projected low-degree cell component

Define the low-degree projection

\[
P_{\le R+1}B
\]

onto the span of

\[
\{1,\xi,\xi^2,\ldots,\xi^{R+1}\}
\]

in \(\ell^2\) over cell centers, preferably with stable orthonormal polynomials.

Then

\[
\|\mu\|_2
\asymp
\|P_{\le R+1}B\|_2
\]

up to conditioning.

This gives the correct bridge:

\[
\boxed{
\text{direct jets}
\Longleftrightarrow
\text{low-degree projected cell component}.
}
\]

The raw cell mass

\[
\|B\|_2^2=\sum_r|B_r|^2
\]

is stronger:

\[
\|B\|_2
\ge
\|P_{\le R+1}B\|_2,
\]

but it can be large entirely in high-degree cell modes invisible to jets.

For the amplitude route, raw cell mass is still useful, because local \(L^2\) over a window \(a\asymp R\) sees frequencies resolved at scale \(1/R\). But if \(M\gg R\), the analysis must use the actual kernel frame, not moment equivalence.

---

# 7. Recommended formulation

The cleanest theorem is not “cell sums are equivalent to jets.” The clean theorem is:

## Theorem A — Cell mass implies local amplitude

Let \(F(t)=\sum_n b_ne^{-itx_n}\), with \(x_n\) grouped into cells \(I_r\) of width \(\delta\asymp a^{-1}\). Define

\[
B_r=\sum_{x_n\in I_r}b_n.
\]

Assume the smoothed Fourier Gram matrix on cell centers has lower frame bound

\[
G_a\ge Q^{-C}a\,I
\]

on the relevant cell span, and intra-cell errors plus dual/background/errors are \(Q^{-C}\)-controlled. Then

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}
\]

implies

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2dt\ge Q^{-A'}
\]

and hence

\[
\sup_{|t|\le a}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A''}.
\]

This is the right sufficient route to \(|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}\).

## Theorem B — Jets correspond to projected cell modes

Let \(M\) cells have centers \(\xi_r\), and let \(\Pi_{\le R+1}\) be the stable polynomial projection. Then

\[
\|\mathcal J_{R+1}F(0)\|
\asymp_{Q^C}
\|\Pi_{\le R+1}B\|_2.
\]

If \(M\le R+2\), this controls the full \(B\). If \(M\gg R\), it controls only the low-degree projection.

---

# 8. Which route to prioritize?

Prioritize **cell mass \(\Rightarrow\) local amplitude**, not exact jet/cell equivalence.

Reason:

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2
\]

is positive and feeds directly into local \(L^2\). It is also the diagnostic Agent 5 can test.

However, for theorem design, avoid a partition with too many cells. The optimal regime is:

\[
\boxed{
M\asymp R
}
\]

or slightly below. This keeps:

\[
\text{enough cells to avoid total-value degeneracy}
\]

while avoiding:

\[
\text{large high-degree nullspace invisible to jets}.
\]

The support width should therefore be roughly

\[
L_{\rm wide}
\asymp
M\frac{Q}{R}
\]

with

\[
M\asymp R^\theta,\qquad 0<\theta\le1,
\]

and the most natural first target is

\[
\boxed{
M\asymp R.
}
\]

Then

\[
L_{\rm wide}\asymp Q.
\]

This means using a macroscopic plateau segment

\[
cQ\le \log n\le (c+c')Q
\]

with \(c+c'<1/2\) to stay away from the saddle.

---

# 9. Final recommendation

Use three related but distinct objects:

1. **Raw cell mass**

   \[
   \sum_r|B_r|^2.
   \]

   Best for proving local \(L^2\) amplitude.

2. **Projected low-degree cell mass**

   \[
   \|\Pi_{\le R+1}B\|_2^2.
   \]

   Best equivalent to jet nonflatness.

3. **Direct jet vector**

   \[
   \left(F^{(k)}(0)/k!\right)_{1\le k\le R+1}.
   \]

   Best for the Wronskian input itself.

For proof strategy, attack:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_r|B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

on a multi-block support with \(M\asymp R\) cells, because it is positive and implies local amplitude.

But recognize:

\[
\boxed{
\text{cell-sum noncancellation is stronger than jet nonflatness in wide supports.}
}
\]

If this theorem is too strong, fall back to the projected version:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\|\Pi_{\le R+1}B(m_0)\|_2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

That projected theorem is essentially equivalent to direct jet nonflatness, but better conditioned for numerical and analytic work.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 06:46:13)

_Thought for 6s_

## Agent 4 / D — Adversarial construction against multiblock cell mass

Executing the assigned Agent 4 task: try to break the multiblock cell-sum theorem

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_r |B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A},
\]

where

\[
B_r(m_0)=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n},
\qquad
x_n=\lambda\log n.
\]

The coordinator prompt asks for an adversarial construction through progressively more realistic layers, and for a verdict on whether the multiblock cell theorem is easier than the full Wronskian incidence theorem. 

---

# 1. Phase-relaxed model: cell theorem is false

In the fully phase-relaxed model, treat

\[
u_n=e^{-im_0\log n}
\]

as independent unit phases.

For a cell \(I_r\), write

\[
B_r=\sum_{n\in I_r} z_n u_n.
\]

The scalar zero condition is

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)\approx \sum_r B_r=0.
\]

The stronger cell-flat event is

\[
B_r=0
\qquad
\forall r.
\]

In the relaxed model, if each cell contains enough terms with comparable magnitudes, we can choose the phases inside each cell so that their polygonal sum vanishes:

\[
\sum_{n\in I_r} z_n u_n=0.
\]

This can be done independently cell by cell.

Thus

\[
B_r=0\quad \forall r
\]

implies

\[
\sum_rB_r=0,
\]

so the \(Z_\alpha=0\) condition is automatically satisfied.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The multiblock cell-sum theorem is false in the phase-relaxed model.}
}
\]

This is expected. The theorem must use actual one-parameter arithmetic, not just coefficient geometry.

---

# 2. Fixed magnitudes: still breakable

Now fix the magnitudes

\[
|z_n|
\]

inside each cell and allow only phases to vary.

A cell sum

\[
B_r=\sum_{n\in I_r}|z_n|e^{i\theta_n}
\]

can be made zero if the largest magnitude in the cell is no larger than the sum of the others:

\[
\max_{n\in I_r}|z_n|
\le
\sum_{n'\in I_r,\ n'\ne n}|z_{n'}|.
\]

For packet-frequency cells with many primes or integers and slowly varying AFE weights, this polygon inequality should hold strongly.

So even with fixed magnitudes,

\[
B_r=0\quad\forall r
\]

is generically feasible in the relaxed phase model.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Fixed magnitudes do not rescue the cell-sum theorem.}
}
\]

The obstruction must be the actual phase law

\[
\theta_n=-m_0\log n,
\]

not magnitude distribution.

---

# 3. Plus branch only: still breakable in relaxed phases

For the plus branch,

\[
B_r^+
=
\sum_{x_n^+\in I_r}
z_n^+(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}.
\]

In a relaxed model with independent phases

\[
u_n^+=e^{-i\theta_n},
\]

each cell can again be balanced independently:

\[
B_r^+=0.
\]

This gives

\[
\sum_rB_r^+=0
\]

and hence the plus-only version of the scalar zero condition.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The plus-only cell theorem is false in relaxed phases.}
}
\]

---

# 4. Reflected dual / folded dual: still breakable in relaxed phases

For the full cell sum,

\[
B_r
=
B_r^+
+
e^{i\Theta_Z}B_r^-
+
B_r^{\rm bg}
+
B_r^E.
\]

Ignoring small background/error first, the relaxed phase model has either:

\[
u_n^+
\quad\text{and}\quad
u_n^-
\]

independent, or the weaker reflected constraint

\[
u_n^-=\overline{u_n^+}.
\]

## Independent dual phases

If \(u^+\) and \(u^-\) are independent, one can choose phases cell by cell to make

\[
B_r^+=0,
\qquad
B_r^-=0,
\]

so

\[
B_r=0.
\]

## Reflected dual phases

If

\[
u_n^-=\overline{u_n^+},
\]

the constraint is stronger. But \(B_r\) is still one complex equation per cell in many phase variables. If each cell contains many terms, the real dimension of phases in a cell is much larger than two. Generically, one can solve

\[
B_r^+
+
e^{i\Theta_Z}B_r^-
+
B_r^{\rm bg}
=0
\]

cell by cell unless the coefficients have a special sign/convexity obstruction.

No such obstruction is visible.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Reflected/folded dual phases still do not kill the relaxed cell-flat construction.}
}
\]

---

# 5. Actual one-parameter phases: first serious obstruction

Now impose the actual orbit:

\[
u_n(m)=e^{-im\log n}.
\]

The cell sums become

\[
B_r(m)
=
\sum_{n\in I_r}z_n e^{-im\log n}.
\]

The cell-flat event is

\[
B_r(m_0)\approx0
\qquad
1\le r\le M.
\]

This is \(M\) complex conditions, or \(2M\) real conditions, on one real parameter \(m_0\), plus possibly \(\alpha\).

For multiblock support with \(K=8\), the number of active cells is on the order of

\[
M\asymp 8
\]

at natural \(1/R\)-cell resolution, and can be larger for \(2R,4R\)-cell diagnostics.

The recurrence cost to hit all cell sums to tolerance \(\varepsilon\) under an independent-value heuristic is roughly

\[
T_{\rm rec}\sim \varepsilon^{-2M}.
\]

For

\[
\varepsilon=Q^{-A},
\]

this gives

\[
T_{\rm rec}\sim Q^{2AM}.
\]

If

\[
M=8,
\]

then

\[
T_{\rm rec}\sim Q^{16A}.
\]

If

\[
M\asymp R,
\]

then

\[
T_{\rm rec}
\sim
Q^{2AR}
=
\exp\!\left(
O\!\left(\frac{(\log Q)^2}{\log\log Q}\right)
\right).
\]

This is substantially more expensive than the scalar incidence condition

\[
Z_\alpha\approx0,\quad \widetilde{\mathcal W}_R\approx0,
\]

which has generic codimension four and recurrence cost only

\[
Q^{O(A)}.
\]

So the first layer where the adversary becomes genuinely constrained is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual one-parameter phase law }u_n=e^{-im\log n}.
}
\]

This is the key reason the multiblock cell theorem is plausibly easier than full Wronskian incidence.

---

# 6. Add \(m>50\): removes trivial corner, not conceptual issue

The condition

\[
m>50
\]

removes artificial small-height or \(m=0\) degeneracies. It prevents the phase vector from being near

\[
u_n=1
\quad\forall n,
\]

which was responsible for some toy corner artifacts in prior determinant tests.

But for the actual one-parameter orbit at large \(m\), the recurrence question remains:

\[
m\mapsto
(e^{-im\log n})_{n\in\mathcal B_{\rm wide}}.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
m>50\text{ is an important numerical admissibility filter, but not a proof mechanism.}
}
\]

---

# 7. Add \(\alpha Q\ll1\): hardest uniform branch

When

\[
\alpha Q\ll1,
\]

the \(\alpha\)-bias in the coefficients is weak:

\[
e^{-\alpha \log n}=1+O(\alpha Q).
\]

So \(\alpha\) does not strongly separate cells or branches.

This is the hardest branch for the cell-sum theorem, because coefficient asymmetry gives less help.

However, even in this hard branch, the multiblock cell-flat condition requires simultaneous vanishing of many cell sums:

\[
B_r(m_0)\approx0
\quad \forall r.
\]

Thus the event remains high codimension along the actual orbit.

Verdict:

\[
\boxed{
\alpha Q\ll1\text{ removes branch/asymmetry help, but cell-flatness is still much higher codimension than scalar }Z_\alpha=0.
}
\]

---

# 8. Recurrence cost comparison

## Full Wronskian incidence

The bad incidence

\[
Z_\alpha(m)\approx0,
\qquad
\widetilde{\mathcal W}_R(m,\alpha)\approx0
\]

is two complex conditions. Generic recurrence cost:

\[
T_{\rm inc}\sim Q^{4A}.
\]

This is too cheap to prove avoidance by codimension alone.

## Multiblock cell-flatness

The bad cell event

\[
B_r(m)\approx0
\quad 1\le r\le M
\]

is \(M\) complex conditions. Generic recurrence cost:

\[
T_{\rm cell}\sim Q^{2AM}.
\]

For \(M=8\),

\[
T_{\rm cell}\sim Q^{16A}.
\]

For \(M\asymp R\),

\[
T_{\rm cell}
\sim
\exp\!\left(
O\left(\frac{(\log Q)^2}{\log\log Q}\right)
\right).
\]

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{multiblock cell-flatness is substantially harder to realize than Wronskian incidence in a recurrence model.}
}
\]

This supports the view that the cell theorem is a smaller, more tractable unlock.

---

# 9. Strongest adversarial mechanism that remains

The only credible remaining adversarial mechanism is actual prime-log recurrence:

\[
m_0\log n
\]

simultaneously arranging phase cancellation inside every cell.

The required theorem to rule this out is:

## Actual-orbit multiblock cell nonrecurrence theorem

Let

\[
B_r(m)
=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m)e^{-im\log n}
+
\text{dual/background/error}.
\]

For admissible \(m,\alpha\) with

\[
Z_\alpha(m)=0,
\]

and for multiblock support with \(M\ge 8\) active cells,

\[
\boxed{
\sum_{r=1}^M |B_r(m)|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Equivalently, the actual orbit avoids the multiblock cell-flat variety

\[
\mathcal C_{\rm flat}
=
\{u:B_r(u)=0\ \forall r\}
\]

at polynomial scale.

This theorem is false in relaxed phase space but plausible on the actual one-parameter orbit.

---

# 10. Verdict

Layer-by-layer:

\[
\begin{array}{c|c}
\text{Layer} & \text{Can adversary force }B_r=0\ \forall r?\\
\hline
\text{phase-relaxed} & \text{yes}\\
\text{fixed magnitudes} & \text{yes, by polygon balancing}\\
\text{plus branch only} & \text{yes}\\
\text{reflected/formal dual} & \text{yes, generically}\\
\text{folded dual} & \text{yes in relaxed phases}\\
\text{actual one-parameter orbit} & \text{first serious obstruction}\\
m>50 & \text{removes corner artifacts only}\\
\alpha Q\ll1 & \text{hard branch, but still high-codimension}
\end{array}
\]

Final conclusion:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The multiblock cell theorem is false in relaxed models but plausibly much easier than full Wronskian incidence on the actual orbit.}
}
\]

The reason is codimension:

\[
\text{cell-flatness imposes }M\text{ complex constraints,}
\]

whereas full Wronskian incidence imposes only two complex constraints.

Thus the next serious theorem target should indeed be:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_r|B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for actual one-parameter prime-log phases on multiblock packet-frequency support.

## Agent 4 / D — Projected cell modes versus raw cell mass

Executing Agent 4’s assigned task: decide whether the theorem should target raw cell mass

\[
\sum_r |B_r|^2
\]

or projected low-degree cell mass

\[
\|\Pi_{\le R+1}B\|_2^2.
\]

The shared prompt asks for a recommendation on which cell theorem should be the proof target in the fixed-width multiblock setting. 

---

# 1. Cell vector and polynomial projection

Let the fixed-width packet-frequency support be

\[
c_-Q\le \log n\le c_+Q,
\qquad
0<c_-<c_+<\frac12,
\]

so that

\[
x_n=\lambda\log n\in[x_-,x_+]\subset(0,1/2).
\]

Partition into cells

\[
I_r,
\qquad
|I_r|\asymp R^{-1},
\qquad
1\le r\le M,
\]

with

\[
M\asymp R.
\]

Define

\[
B_r
=
\sum_{x_n\in I_r}
z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}
\]

or, canonically,

\[
B_r
=
B_r^+
+
e^{i\Theta_Z}B_r^-
+
B_r^{\rm bg}
+
B_r^E.
\]

Let

\[
B=(B_1,\ldots,B_M)\in\mathbb C^M.
\]

Choose a stable orthonormal basis

\[
\phi_0,\ldots,\phi_{R+1}
\]

on the cell centers \(\xi_r\), with respect to weights \(w_r\asymp |I_r|\) or coefficient-adapted weights. Define

\[
(\Pi_{\le R+1}B)_j
=
\sum_{r=1}^{M} w_r B_r\overline{\phi_j(\xi_r)},
\qquad
0\le j\le R+1.
\]

This projection is the cell-coordinate version of the low-degree jet content.

---

# 2. Relation to jets

The jet moments are

\[
\mu_k
=
\sum_n b_n x_n^k,
\qquad
0\le k\le R+1,
\]

where

\[
b_n=z_n(\alpha,m_0)e^{-im_0\log n}.
\]

Approximating \(x_n\) by the cell center \(\xi_r\),

\[
\mu_k
=
\sum_r B_r \xi_r^k
+
\mathrm{Err}_{k,\mathrm{intra}}.
\]

Equivalently, in a stable polynomial basis,

\[
\mu_\phi
=
\Pi_{\le R+1}B
+
\mathrm{Err}_{\mathrm{intra}}.
\]

Thus

\[
\boxed{
\|\Pi_{\le R+1}B\|_2
\asymp
\|\mathcal J_{\le R+1}F(0)\|_2
}
\]

up to stable-basis conditioning and intra-cell Taylor errors.

So projected cell mass is the correct object if the goal is direct jet nonflatness:

\[
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

---

# 3. Raw cell mass versus projected cell mass

Raw cell mass is

\[
\|B\|_2^2
=
\sum_r |B_r|^2.
\]

Projected cell mass is

\[
\|\Pi_{\le R+1}B\|_2^2.
\]

Since \(\Pi_{\le R+1}\) is an orthogonal projection,

\[
\|\Pi_{\le R+1}B\|_2
\le
\|B\|_2.
\]

Therefore

\[
\boxed{
\text{raw cell mass lower bound is stronger than projected cell mass lower bound.}
}
\]

But raw mass is also more directly tied to local \(L^2\) amplitude. If \(a\asymp R\), then

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2\,dt
\]

approximately resolves cells of width \(R^{-1}\). With a smoothed window,

\[
\int |W_a(t)|\,|F(t)|^2dt
\approx
a\,B^*G_aB,
\]

where \(G_a\) is the cell Fourier Gram matrix. Under a lower frame bound,

\[
G_a\ge Q^{-C}I,
\]

one obtains

\[
\int_{-a}^{a}|F(t)|^2dt
\ge
Q^{-C}a\sum_r|B_r|^2
-
\mathrm{Err}.
\]

Thus

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}
\]

directly implies local amplitude.

Projected mass, by contrast, only controls the low-degree polynomial component of \(B\). It can be large enough to imply jets, but it does not automatically give full local \(L^2\) amplitude unless the window/kernel is restricted to low-degree modes.

---

# 4. When raw cell mass is too strong

If

\[
M\gg R,
\]

then \(B\) has many high-degree cell modes invisible to jets through order \(R+1\). In that regime:

\[
\|B\|_2\gg0
\]

can hold while

\[
\Pi_{\le R+1}B\approx0.
\]

So raw cell mass may detect oscillatory high-frequency cell structure that does not help the Wronskian jet input.

However, in the proposed fixed-width support with cell width \(R^{-1}\),

\[
M\asymp R.
\]

This is the good regime. Here the low-degree space has dimension \(R+2\), comparable to or larger than the cell count. If the basis is well conditioned, then

\[
\Pi_{\le R+1}
\]

is close to full-rank on the cell space. In the ideal case

\[
M\le R+2,
\]

the projection is injective and

\[
\|B\|_2
\le Q^C \|\Pi_{\le R+1}B\|_2.
\]

Therefore, in the intended regime

\[
M\asymp R,
\]

raw and projected cell mass are polynomially comparable if the cell centers and basis are stable.

---

# 5. Clean theorem options

## Option A — Raw cell mass theorem

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_r|B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This gives the clean implication

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2\ge Q^{-A}
\Rightarrow
\int_{-R}^{R}|F(t)|^2dt\ge Q^{-A'}
\Rightarrow
\sup_{|t|\le R}|F(t)|\ge Q^{-A''}
\Rightarrow
|\mathbf z'|\ge Q^{-A'''}.
\]

Advantages:

\[
\boxed{
\text{positive, numerically stable, directly tied to }L^2\text{ amplitude.}
}
\]

Disadvantage:

\[
\boxed{
\text{stronger than necessary if }M\gg R.
}
\]

## Option B — Projected cell mass theorem

\[
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\|\Pi_{\le R+1}B(m_0)\|_2^2\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This gives directly

\[
|\mathbf z'(m_0,\alpha)|\ge Q^{-A'}
\]

through jet equivalence.

Advantages:

\[
\boxed{
\text{closest to the exact Wronskian input.}
}
\]

Disadvantage:

\[
\boxed{
\text{less positive/physical than raw }L^2\text{ cell mass.}
}
\]

It also may be harder to prove by \(L^2\) window methods, because it asks specifically for noncancellation in the low-degree polynomial projection.

## Option C — Two-tier theorem

Prove raw cell mass in the intended regime

\[
M\asymp R,
\]

and include a fallback projected version for wider supports:

\[
M\gg R.
\]

This is the strongest practical formulation.

---

# 6. Recommendation

The proof target should be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove raw cell mass for }M\asymp R.
}
\]

That is,

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\sum_{r=1}^{M}|B_r(m_0)|^2\ge Q^{-A},
\qquad
M\asymp R.
}
\]

Use fixed-width support

\[
c_-Q\le\log n\le c_+Q,
\qquad
0<c_-<c_+<1/2,
\]

and choose cell width so that

\[
M\asymp R.
\]

This theorem is strong enough to imply local amplitude and therefore \(|\mathbf z'|\), while not being unnecessarily stronger than the jet theorem because \(M\) is only comparable to \(R\).

For theorem robustness, also record the projected fallback:

\[
\boxed{
Z_\alpha(m_0)=0
\Rightarrow
\|\Pi_{\le R+1}B(m_0)\|_2^2\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This projected version is the exact coordinate form of direct jet nonflatness.

---

# 7. Final answer

\[
\boxed{
\text{Primary target: raw cell mass on }M\asymp R\text{ cells.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Fallback/diagnostic target: projected low-degree cell mass.}
}
\]

Raw cell mass is best for proving polylog-local amplitude:

\[
\sum_r|B_r|^2
\Rightarrow
\sup_{|t|\le R}|Z_\alpha(m_0+\lambda t)|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Projected cell mass is best for matching the Wronskian input:

\[
\|\Pi_{\le R+1}B\|_2
\Longleftrightarrow
|\mathbf z'|.
\]

In the intended fixed-width regime with

\[
M\asymp R,
\]

the two should be polynomially comparable under stable basis conditioning. So the recommended theorem statement should include both, with raw cell mass as the main route and projected mass as the fallback if raw cell noncancellation proves too strong.
