_Thought for 10s_

## Continuing attack — split D4 into linear, nonlinear, and first-entry anisotropy branches

The remaining D4 risk is now precise:

\[
\mathbf A_{\rm row}
=
q_0u^3\left(L\Gamma+\mathcal Q_{\ge2}(\Gamma)\right)+\mathbf E,
\qquad
\Gamma=(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2}).
\]

If \(\mathcal Q_{\ge2}=0\) or is perturbative, D4 closes by the linear matrix \(L\). If \(\mathcal Q_{\ge2}\) is same-scale, D4 becomes a finite nonlinear row-polynomial observability problem. The current downstream update already identifies the row matrix normal form and correction control as the finite local task after Wall-Escape Sampling. 

---

# 1. Normalize the row polynomial

After extracting the common factor \(q_0u^3\), write

\[
\mathbf B(\Gamma;Y)
:=
\frac{\mathbf A_{\rm row}}{q_0u^3}
=
L(Y)\Gamma
+
Q_2(Y)[\Gamma,\Gamma]
+
Q_3(Y)[\Gamma,\Gamma,\Gamma]
+\cdots
+
E(Y).
\]

Here \(Y\) denotes controlled local variables:

\[
Y=(q_0,u,q_0',u',\ldots).
\]

The linear theorem is:

\[
\sigma_2(L(Y))\ge Q^{-C_L}.
\]

The nonlinear obstruction is:

\[
L\Gamma+Q_2(\Gamma,\Gamma)+\cdots\approx0
\]

for nonzero \(\Gamma\).

So the corrected D4 target is:

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf B(\Gamma;Y)|\ge Q^{-C}|\Gamma|
}
\]

on the D4 admissible set.

If this holds, then

\[
|\mathbf A_{\rm row}|
\ge
|q_0||u|^3Q^{-C}|\Gamma|,
\]

and the previous D4 lower bound follows.

---

# 2. Small-anisotropy branch

Let

\[
|\Gamma|\le \gamma_+.
\]

Assume polynomial coefficient bounds:

\[
\|Q_j(Y)\|\le Q^{C_j}.
\]

Then

\[
|Q_{\ge2}(\Gamma)|
\le
Q^{C_2}|\Gamma|^2+Q^{C_3}|\Gamma|^3+\cdots.
\]

If

\[
\gamma_+\le cQ^{-C_2-C_L},
\]

then

\[
|Q_{\ge2}(\Gamma)|
\le
\frac12\sigma_2(L)|\Gamma|.
\]

Therefore

\[
|L\Gamma+Q_{\ge2}(\Gamma)|
\ge
\frac12\sigma_2(L)|\Gamma|.
\]

So D4 closes in the small-anisotropy branch.

This branch is clean:

\[
\boxed{
|\Gamma|\le Q^{-C_\Gamma}
\Rightarrow
\text{linear D4 works}.
}
\]

---

# 3. Large-anisotropy branch

Now suppose

\[
|\Gamma|>\gamma_+.
\]

There are two possible attacks.

## 3.1 First-entry anisotropy sampling

If Gate 2 mass is carried at large anisotropy, move to the first point where

\[
|\Gamma|=\gamma_+.
\]

At that first-entry point, anisotropy is exactly controlled:

\[
|\Gamma|=\gamma_+,
\]

so the small-anisotropy D4 branch applies.

This requires:

1. a connected path in \(I\) from a smaller-\(\Gamma\) point to the large-\(\Gamma\) point;
2. \(q_0\) and \(u\) remain live along the move;
3. row conversion is existential in \(I\).

If those hold, large anisotropy is reduced to controlled anisotropy.

## 3.2 Nonlinear row-polynomial observability

If first-entry sampling is unavailable, prove directly:

\[
\boxed{
|L\Gamma+Q_{\ge2}(\Gamma)|\ge Q^{-C}|\Gamma|
}
\]

for all admissible \(\Gamma\).

This is finite algebra. Homogenize by writing

\[
\Gamma=r\omega,
\qquad
r=|\Gamma|,
\qquad
|\omega|=1.
\]

Then

\[
\mathbf B
=
r\left[
L\omega
+
rQ_2(\omega,\omega)
+
r^2Q_3(\omega,\omega,\omega)
+\cdots
\right].
\]

Need

\[
\left|
L\omega
+
rQ_2(\omega,\omega)
+\cdots
\right|
\ge Q^{-C}
\]

for

\[
r\in[\gamma_+,Q^{C_\Gamma}],
\quad
|\omega|=1.
\]

The bad set is finite-dimensional:

\[
\mathcal Z_{\rm row}
=
\{(Y,r,\omega):L\omega+rQ_2(\omega,\omega)+\cdots=0,\ |\omega|=1\}.
\]

If the polynomial map has no admissible common root, compactness plus quantitative coefficient bounds should give a polynomial lower bound. If roots exist, one must add more rows.

---

# 4. Row-packet first-entry theorem

The first-entry approach is more compatible with the existing wall-escape strategy.

Define

\[
r(m)=|\Gamma(m)|.
\]

Fix

\[
\gamma_-<\gamma_+.
\]

Gate 2 pointwise mass gives

\[
|u|^2r^2\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

If \(r\) is very large, then \(u\) may be small, but if \(r\le Q^{C_\Gamma}\), still

\[
|u|\ge Q^{-A/2-C_\Gamma}.
\]

So \(u\) is polynomially live.

Suppose a point \(m_0\) has

\[
r(m_0)>\gamma_+.
\]

If in the same component of the source-live set there is a point with

\[
r<\gamma_+,
\]

then by continuity there is

\[
m_1
\]

with

\[
r(m_1)=\gamma_+.
\]

At \(m_1\), if \(q_0\) is non-wall, D4 applies directly. If \(q_0\) is wall, combine with wall escape.

Thus large-anisotropy mass can be moved to controlled-anisotropy mass unless the entire source-live component satisfies

\[
|\Gamma|\ge \gamma_+.
\]

If that happens, row conversion may be even easier if the full nonlinear row polynomial is observable.

---

# 5. Combine wall escape and anisotropy first-entry

We now have two escape hypersurfaces:

\[
|q_0|=\varepsilon
\]

and

\[
|\Gamma|=\gamma_+.
\]

A robust sampling algorithm is:

1. Start from a Gate 2-live point:

\[
G=|u|^2|\Gamma|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

2. If \(|q_0|\ge\varepsilon\) and \(|\Gamma|\le\gamma_+\), apply linear D4.

3. If \(|q_0|<\varepsilon\), use wall escape to reach \(|q_0|=\varepsilon\) with \(u,\Gamma_G\) live.

4. If \(|\Gamma|>\gamma_+\), use anisotropy first-entry to reach \(|\Gamma|=\gamma_+\), provided \(q_0,u\) stay live.

5. If first-entry is impossible because \(|\Gamma|\ge\gamma_+\) on a whole component, use nonlinear row-polynomial observability on that component.

Thus the only genuinely new branch is the full nonlinear row observability on a large-anisotropy component.

---

# 6. Finite nonlinear row observability theorem

Let

\[
\mathbf A_N
=
q_0u^3\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)+\mathbf E_N,
\]

where

\[
\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)
=
L_N(Y)\Gamma+Q_{\ge2,N}(Y,\Gamma).
\]

A robust theorem is:

## Nonlinear Row Observability

For the finite row family \(A_7,A_9,A_{11},A_{13}\), there exists \(C\) such that

\[
|\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)|
\ge Q^{-C}|\Gamma|
\]

for all admissible \(Y,\Gamma\) satisfying Gate 2 bounds.

Then, with

\[
|\mathbf E_N|\le \frac12|q_0||u|^3Q^{-C}|\Gamma|,
\]

D4 closes in all anisotropy regimes.

This theorem is finite. It can be checked by searching for common zeros of \(\mathbf P_N\) on the projective \(\Gamma\)-line.

Since \(\Gamma\in\mathbb R^2\) or \(\mathbb C^2\), projectivize by

\[
z=\Gamma_{G,2}/\Gamma_G
\]

when \(\Gamma_G\ne0\). Then row equations become finite polynomial equations in \(z\) and \(r=|\Gamma|\). A common root indicates a genuine row-null anisotropy direction.

---

# 7. If nonlinear observability fails

If there is an admissible root

\[
\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)=0,\qquad \Gamma\ne0,
\]

then D4 fails for this row family. Options:

1. Add more rows \(A_{15},A_{17}\).
2. Modify Gate 2 anisotropy to match row-observed directions.
3. Use first-entry to avoid the root variety.
4. Use row derivatives / mobility to escape the root.

This is analogous to the \(q_0\)-wall escape. If the row polynomial vanishes only on a lower-dimensional variety, existential sampling may avoid it.

---

# 8. Updated D4 proof strategy

The D4 proof should proceed in layers:

## Layer 1 — linear small-anisotropy

Prove

\[
\sigma_2(L_N)\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

Then D4 closes for

\[
|\Gamma|\le\gamma_+.
\]

## Layer 2 — anisotropy first-entry

Show large-\(\Gamma\) Gate 2 mass can be sampled at

\[
|\Gamma|=\gamma_+.
\]

Then reduce to Layer 1.

## Layer 3 — nonlinear observability

If first-entry fails, prove the full polynomial row map is nonzero on the large-\(\Gamma\) component.

## Layer 4 — enlarge rows if needed

If nonlinear observability fails, add rows.

---

# 9. Main new theorem to formulate

## Controlled-Anisotropy Sampling Theorem

Assume Gate 2 mass and row conversion is existential on \(I\). Then either:

1. there exists a D4 sample with

\[
|q_0|\ge Q^{-C},
\quad
|u|^2|\Gamma|^2\ge Q^{-C},
\quad
|\Gamma|\le\gamma_+,
\]

or

2. Gate 2 mass lies on a large-anisotropy component where nonlinear row observability must be invoked.

This theorem isolates the only remaining nonlinear branch.

---

# 10. Attack conclusion

The D4 correction problem splits into:

\[
\boxed{
\text{linear D4 for controlled anisotropy}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite nonlinear row observability for large anisotropy}.
}
\]

The likely situation is that same-order nonlinear anisotropy terms are absent, in which case controlled-anisotropy sampling is unnecessary and linear D4 closes outright.

But if nonlinear terms exist, the attack above gives a finite way to handle them.

The next exact audit is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Are there same-order terms }q_0u^3\Gamma^2\text{ in }A_7,A_9?
}
\]

If no: D4 closes by the linear determinant.

If yes: use controlled-anisotropy sampling or nonlinear row observability.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 11:30:08)

_Thought for 9s_

## Continuing attack — eliminate the large-anisotropy branch by row-polynomial dominance

The last remaining D4 ambiguity is whether same-order nonlinear anisotropy terms appear:

\[
\mathbf A_{\rm row}
=
q_0u^3\left(L\Gamma+Q_2(\Gamma,\Gamma)+Q_{\ge3}(\Gamma)\right)+\mathbf E.
\]

If no such terms appear, D4 closes by the linear determinant. If they do appear, we need to show they do not create common row cancellation. I’ll now attack the harder case directly.

---

# 1. Normalize by anisotropy size

Let

\[
\Gamma=(\Gamma_1,\Gamma_2)
=
(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2}),
\]

and write

\[
r=|\Gamma|,
\qquad
\omega=\Gamma/r,
\qquad
|\omega|=1.
\]

Then

\[
L\Gamma+Q_2(\Gamma,\Gamma)+Q_3(\Gamma,\Gamma,\Gamma)+\cdots
\]

becomes

\[
r\left[
L\omega
+
r Q_2(\omega,\omega)
+
r^2 Q_3(\omega,\omega,\omega)
+\cdots
\right].
\]

Define the projective row polynomial

\[
P(Y,r,\omega)
=
L(Y)\omega
+
r Q_2(Y;\omega,\omega)
+
r^2 Q_3(Y;\omega,\omega,\omega)
+\cdots.
\]

Then D4 requires

\[
\boxed{
|P(Y,r,\omega)|\ge Q^{-C}
}
\]

on the admissible set.

If this holds, then

\[
|\mathbf A_{\rm row}|
\ge
|q_0||u|^3rQ^{-C}
-
|\mathbf E|.
\]

With half-leading residual control, D4 closes.

---

# 2. Small-\(r\) branch is already solved

If

\[
r=|\Gamma|\le \gamma_+,
\]

then

\[
|rQ_2+r^2Q_3+\cdots|
\le
Q^C\gamma_+.
\]

Choose

\[
\gamma_+\le c Q^{-C-C_L}
\]

where

\[
\sigma_2(L)\ge Q^{-C_L}.
\]

Then

\[
|P(Y,r,\omega)|
\ge
|L\omega|-|rQ_2+\cdots|
\ge
\frac12 Q^{-C_L}.
\]

So the small-anisotropy branch is done.

The hard branch is

\[
|\Gamma|>\gamma_+.
\]

---

# 3. Large anisotropy forces either first-entry or nonlinear observability

Suppose Gate 2 gives a point with

\[
G=|u|^2|\Gamma|^2\ge Q^{-A},
\]

and

\[
|\Gamma|>\gamma_+.
\]

If the connected source-live component contains a point with

\[
|\Gamma|\le\gamma_+,
\]

then by continuity there is a first-entry point

\[
m_1
\]

with

\[
|\Gamma(m_1)|=\gamma_+.
\]

At \(m_1\), the small-\(r\) branch applies, provided

\[
u,\quad q_0
\]

remain polynomially non-small. If \(q_0\) is small, use the already established wall-escape first-exit step. If \(u\) threatens to drop, use the same Lipschitz/mobility logic used in wall escape.

Thus large anisotropy only remains a problem on components where

\[
|\Gamma|\ge \gamma_+
\]

throughout the relevant component.

On such a component, row conversion must use full nonlinear observability.

---

# 4. Nonlinear row observability is a finite projective condition

For a fixed local state \(Y\), consider

\[
P(Y,r,\omega)
\]

with

\[
r\in[\gamma_+,Q^{C_\Gamma}],
\qquad
|\omega|=1.
\]

The bad set is

\[
P(Y,r,\omega)=0.
\]

Since \(\omega\in\mathbb P^1\), this is a finite projective algebra problem once the row formulas are known.

Use an affine chart:

\[
z=\Gamma_2/\Gamma_1
\]

when \(\Gamma_1\neq0\). Then

\[
\omega\sim(1,z),
\]

and each row equation becomes a polynomial/rational equation in

\[
z,\quad r,\quad Y.
\]

The finite test is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Do the row polynomials have a common admissible root in }(z,r)?
}
\]

If no, compactness plus polynomial coefficient bounds should give

\[
|P(Y,r,\omega)|\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

If yes, add more rows or change the Gate 2 anisotropy to the row-observed combination.

---

# 5. A useful sufficient condition: leading homogeneous dominance

Let

\[
d
\]

be the largest anisotropy degree appearing at the same row order.

Write

\[
P(Y,r,\omega)
=
L\omega+rQ_2+\cdots+r^{d-1}Q_d.
\]

If the top homogeneous term satisfies

\[
|Q_d(Y;\omega^d)|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

for every \(|\omega|=1\), then for sufficiently large \(r\), the row polynomial is nonzero.

More precisely, if

\[
r^{d-1}|Q_d|\gg
|L|+r|Q_2|+\cdots+r^{d-2}|Q_{d-1}|,
\]

then

\[
|P|\ge Q^{-C}r^{d-1}.
\]

Thus large anisotropy can be controlled either by the linear term or by the top nonlinear term.

The only dangerous regime is where two homogeneous layers cancel:

\[
L\omega+rQ_2+\cdots\approx0.
\]

That again is finite algebra.

---

# 6. Row-pair nonvanishing via resultants

For two rows, the projective equations are

\[
P_1(z,r)=0,
\]

\[
P_2(z,r)=0.
\]

Eliminate \(z\) by the resultant:

\[
\operatorname{Res}_z(P_1,P_2)(r).
\]

If

\[
\operatorname{Res}_z(P_1,P_2)
\]

is not identically zero, then common roots occur only at finitely many \(r\)-values.

Then one can either:

1. prove these roots are outside the admissible interval

\[
[\gamma_+,Q^{C_\Gamma}],
\]

or

2. use first-entry / escape in \(r\)-space to avoid them existentially, or

3. add a third row.

For the extended row set, common roots require all pairwise resultants to vanish consistently. This becomes increasingly unlikely and is finite to test.

Thus the nonlinear large-anisotropy branch can be reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{row-polynomial resultant nonvanishing}.
}
\]

---

# 7. Add rows to kill nonlinear common roots

If \(A_7,A_9\) have a common nonlinear row-polynomial root, add

\[
A_{11},A_{13}.
\]

Define

\[
P_N(Y,r,\omega)
=
(P_7,P_9,P_{11},P_{13}).
\]

Need

\[
|P_N|\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

A common root now satisfies four equations in the two variables

\[
(r,z)
\]

for fixed \(Y\). Generically impossible.

Finite audit:

1. compute \(P_7,P_9,P_{11},P_{13}\);
2. compute common-zero ideal;
3. prove it has no admissible solutions, or find the residual bad variety in \(Y\).

If a bad variety in \(Y\) remains, use mobility/escape in \(Y(m)\), just as for \(q_0\) and \(|\Gamma|\).

---

# 8. A hierarchy of escape surfaces

The downstream row conversion now has a systematic structure.

Bad surfaces:

\[
q_0=0
\]

is handled by wall escape.

\[
|\Gamma|=\infty\text{ or too large}
\]

is handled by anisotropy first-entry or nonlinear observability.

\[
\det L=0
\]

is handled by adding rows or determinant escape.

\[
P_N=0
\]

is handled by adding rows or row-polynomial escape.

This suggests a general principle:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If a bad set is defined by a finite row polynomial and sampling is existential,}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{then either add enough rows to make it empty or use local escape from its sublevel set.}
}
\]

This is much stronger than the original fixed-point wall concern.

---

# 9. Revised D4 theorem including nonlinear terms

## Full Row-Polynomial D4 Theorem

Let

\[
\mathbf A_N
=
q_0u^3\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)+\mathbf E_N.
\]

Assume on the D4 admissible set

\[
|\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)|\ge Q^{-C_P}|\Gamma|.
\]

Assume

\[
|\mathbf E_N|\le
\frac12|q_0||u|^3Q^{-C_P}|\Gamma|.
\]

Then

\[
|\mathbf A_N|\ge
\frac12|q_0||u|^3Q^{-C_P}|\Gamma|.
\]

With

\[
|q_0|\ge Q^{-C_q},
\]

\[
|u|^2|\Gamma|^2\ge Q^{-A_2},
\]

and either \(|u|\ge Q^{-C_u}\) or \(|\Gamma|\le Q^{C_\Gamma}\), this gives

\[
|\mathbf A_N|\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

This theorem subsumes the linear D4 theorem. The linear case is simply

\[
\mathbf P_N=L_N\Gamma.
\]

---

# 10. Attack conclusion

Same-order nonlinear anisotropy terms do not necessarily break D4. They convert D4 from linear observability

\[
\sigma_2(L)\ge Q^{-C}
\]

to finite row-polynomial observability

\[
|\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)|\ge Q^{-C}|\Gamma|.
\]

This is still finite and local.

The next concrete audit is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Determine whether same-order nonlinear anisotropy terms exist.}
}
\]

If no, D4 closes by the linear determinant.

If yes, compute the finite row-polynomial map \(P_N\) and test common-root/resultant nonvanishing.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 11:31:11)

_Thought for 10s_

## Agent 4 / D — Continue attack: row-polynomial observability via projective reduction

Continuing the D4 attack. The prior reduction was:

\[
\mathbf A_N
=
q_0u^3\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)+\mathbf E_N,
\qquad
\Gamma=(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2}),
\]

and D4 closes if

\[
|\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)|\ge Q^{-C}|\Gamma|
\]

on the D4 admissible set. This covers both the linear case and the possible nonlinear-anisotropy case. The current downstream architecture already places D4 after Gate 2 and Wall-Escape Sampling. 

---

# 1. Projectivize the anisotropy variable

Set

\[
r=|\Gamma|,
\qquad
\omega=\Gamma/r,
\qquad
|\omega|=1.
\]

Then

\[
\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)
=
r\,\mathbf p_N(Y,r,\omega),
\]

where

\[
\mathbf p_N(Y,r,\omega)
=
L_N(Y)\omega
+
rQ_{2,N}(Y;\omega,\omega)
+
r^2Q_{3,N}(Y;\omega,\omega,\omega)
+\cdots.
\]

So the D4 observability condition becomes

\[
\boxed{
|\mathbf p_N(Y,r,\omega)|\ge Q^{-C}
}
\]

for

\[
r\in[r_-,r_+],
\qquad
|\omega|=1,
\]

where \(r_-\) is the lower anisotropy threshold from Gate 2 sampling and \(r_+\) is the polynomial upper bound for anisotropy.

---

# 2. Reduce to one complex/projective coordinate

If \(\Gamma_G\neq0\), write

\[
z=\frac{\Gamma_{G,2}}{\Gamma_G}.
\]

Then

\[
\Gamma=\Gamma_G(1,z).
\]

Every row polynomial becomes a finite polynomial/rational expression

\[
P_k(Y,r,z).
\]

D4 fails only if there exists

\[
(r,z)
\]

in the admissible range such that

\[
P_7(Y,r,z)=P_9(Y,r,z)=\cdots=P_{2N+1}(Y,r,z)=0.
\]

Thus the finite algebraic obstruction is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Z_N(Y)
=
\{(r,z):P_k(Y,r,z)=0\ \forall k\}
\neq\varnothing.
}
\]

If

\[
\mathcal Z_N(Y)=\varnothing
\]

on the admissible set and coefficients are polynomially bounded, then compactness plus quantitative algebraic lower bounds gives

\[
|\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)|\ge Q^{-C}|\Gamma|.
\]

---

# 3. Two-row resultant test

For \(A_7,A_9\), compute

\[
P_7(Y,r,z),\qquad P_9(Y,r,z).
\]

The common-root obstruction is detected by

\[
\operatorname{Res}_z(P_7,P_9)(Y,r).
\]

If

\[
\operatorname{Res}_z(P_7,P_9)
\not\equiv0,
\]

then common roots occur only at finitely many values of \(r\), determined by

\[
\operatorname{Res}_z(P_7,P_9)(Y,r)=0.
\]

Then D4 can be closed in one of three ways:

1. prove this resultant has no roots in the admissible interval

\[
r\in[r_-,r_+];
\]

2. use first-entry / escape in \(r\)-space to avoid the root levels;

3. add \(A_{11}\) or \(A_{13}\) and require the third row to be nonzero at the remaining common roots.

Thus even when nonlinear terms exist, the obstruction is finite.

---

# 4. Three-row common-root kill

If \(P_7,P_9\) have a common root, add \(P_{11}\).

The bad set becomes

\[
P_7=P_9=P_{11}=0.
\]

In variables \((r,z)\), three equations are generically overdetermined.

A finite certificate is:

\[
1\in (P_7,P_9,P_{11})
\]

in the localized algebra over the admissible coefficient ring, or more concretely:

\[
B_7P_7+B_9P_9+B_{11}P_{11}=1
\]

with polynomially bounded coefficients on the admissible region.

A weaker but sufficient analytic certificate is:

\[
|P_7|^2+|P_9|^2+|P_{11}|^2\ge Q^{-C}
\]

for all admissible \((r,z,Y)\).

This is exactly the row-polynomial observability theorem.

---

# 5. If the row polynomials share a structural factor

Suppose every row polynomial shares a factor

\[
S(Y,r,z),
\]

so

\[
P_k=S\widetilde P_k.
\]

Then the rows vanish whenever

\[
S=0.
\]

There are two possibilities.

## Case A — \(S\) is harmless

If

\[
|S|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

on the Gate 2 admissible set, absorb \(S\) into the lower bound.

## Case B — \(S=0\) defines a real secondary wall

Then either:

1. prove a secondary escape theorem from \(|S|\ll1\);
2. add rows not divisible by \(S\);
3. change the row invariant.

This mirrors the \(q_0\)-wall. The key is that such a factor is now explicit and finite.

---

# 6. Escape from a secondary row-polynomial wall

Let

\[
S(m)
\]

be a scalar obstruction such as

\[
\det L(Y(m)),
\]

or a resultant

\[
\operatorname{Res}_z(P_7,P_9)(Y(m),r(m)).
\]

If D4 samples fall where

\[
|S|\ll1,
\]

but

\[
|S'|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

or

\[
|S''|\ge Q^{-C},
\]

then by the same first-exit method there exists a nearby point where

\[
|S|\ge Q^{-C'}
\]

while the Gate 2 signal remains live.

Thus all secondary algebraic walls can be treated by the same paradigm:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either add rows, or escape from the sublevel set.}
}
\]

This suggests a robust finite algorithm for D4:

- check row-polynomial common roots;
- if common roots exist, identify the scalar defining factors;
- either add rows or prove sublevel escape for those factors.

---

# 7. Back to the likely simple case

The displayed leading architecture strongly suggests no nonlinear problem is needed:

\[
A_7\sim H\Gamma_G,
\qquad
A_9\sim H\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

If those are true leading rows, then

\[
P_7=-\frac{c_7}{288}\Gamma_G,
\]

\[
P_9=-\frac{c_9}{288}\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

In projective coordinates

\[
P_7=-\frac{c_7}{288},
\]

\[
P_9=-\frac{c_9}{288}z.
\]

These never vanish simultaneously if

\[
c_7c_9\ne0.
\]

Indeed, in the chart \(\Gamma_G\neq0\), \(P_7\neq0\). In the other chart \(\Gamma_{G,2}\neq0\), \(P_9\neq0\).

So the expected row-polynomial observability certificate is trivial:

\[
\boxed{
|P_7|^2+|P_9|^2\ge c>0.
}
\]

Everything else is residual error control.

---

# 8. D4 final finite certificate

The final certificate should be stated as:

## Row-Polynomial Observability Certificate

There exists a finite row set

\[
\{A_7,A_9,\ldots,A_{2N+1}\}
\]

and a row-polynomial expansion

\[
\mathbf A_N
=
q_0u^3\left[
r\,\mathbf p_N(Y,r,\omega)
\right]
+
\mathbf E_N,
\]

such that

\[
|\mathbf p_N(Y,r,\omega)|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

for all admissible

\[
Y,\quad r\in[r_-,r_+],\quad |\omega|=1,
\]

and

\[
|\mathbf E_N|
\le
\frac12|q_0||u|^3rQ^{-C}.
\]

Then

\[
|\mathbf A_N|\ge Q^{-C'}.
\]

With row mobility,

\[
\int_I|\mathbf A_N|^2\ge Q^{-C''}|I|.
\]

This closes D4.

---

# 9. What to compute next

The next executable audit is:

1. compute the row-polynomial principal parts

\[
P_7,P_9
\]

after extracting \(q_0u^3\);

2. verify whether

\[
P_7=-c_7\Gamma_G/288,
\qquad
P_9=-c_9\Gamma_{G,2}/288
\]

up to lower-order terms;

3. if nonlinear terms are present, compute

\[
\operatorname{Res}_z(P_7,P_9);
\]

4. if the resultant has admissible roots, add

\[
P_{11},P_{13};
\]

5. prove the final lower bound

\[
|\mathbf p_N|\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

This is finite algebra.

---

# 10. Attack conclusion

D4 has been reduced from “correction control” to a concrete row-polynomial observability certificate.

Expected outcome:

\[
A_7,A_9
\]

already work, because they measure \(\Gamma_G\) and \(\Gamma_{G,2}\) separately.

If not, the finite projective/resultant audit tells us exactly what row to add or what secondary wall to escape.

This is now a tractable local algebra problem rather than a global analytic obstruction.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 11:32:23)

_Thought for 17s_

## Continuing attack — make D4 row-polynomial observability into a Nullstellensatz certificate

The D4 row-conversion problem has now been reduced to a finite algebraic statement:

\[
\mathbf A_N
=
q_0u^3\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)+\mathbf E_N,
\qquad
\Gamma=(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2}),
\]

and we need

\[
|\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)|\ge Q^{-C}|\Gamma|
\]

on the D4 admissible set. The expected easy case is still

\[
A_7\sim H\Gamma_G,\qquad A_9\sim H\Gamma_{G,2},
\]

which would make \(A_7,A_9\) enough. But I’ll now attack the harder case where nonlinear anisotropy terms exist. This turns D4 into a finite ideal-certificate problem. The downstream framework has already identified D4 as the matrix row-conversion/correction-control layer after Gate 2 and Wall-Escape Sampling. 

---

# 1. Homogenize the row polynomial

Let

\[
\Gamma=r\omega,\qquad r=|\Gamma|,\qquad |\omega|=1.
\]

Write

\[
\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)
=
r\,\mathbf p_N(Y,r,\omega).
\]

D4 needs

\[
|\mathbf p_N(Y,r,\omega)|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

for

\[
r\in[r_-,r_+],
\]

where

\[
r_-=Q^{-C_-}
\]

comes from Gate 2 sampling, and

\[
r_+=Q^{C_+}
\]

comes from polynomial anisotropy upper bounds.

Use projective coordinate

\[
z=\frac{\Gamma_{G,2}}{\Gamma_G}
\]

on the chart \(\Gamma_G\ne0\). Then every row becomes a polynomial or rational function

\[
p_k(Y,r,z).
\]

After clearing harmless denominators, D4 observability is:

\[
\boxed{
(p_7,p_9,\ldots,p_{2N+1})
\text{ have no common admissible zero in }(r,z).
}
\]

---

# 2. Nullstellensatz certificate

A proof-level finite certificate is:

\[
\boxed{
1=\sum_k B_k(Y,r,z)\,p_k(Y,r,z)
}
\]

on the admissible chart, after localizing away from known harmless denominators and constraints such as \(\Gamma_G\ne0\).

If the coefficients satisfy polynomial bounds

\[
|B_k|\le Q^C
\]

on the admissible region, then

\[
1
\le
\sum_k |B_k||p_k|
\le
Q^C\sum_k |p_k|.
\]

Therefore

\[
\max_k |p_k|\ge Q^{-C'}.
\]

This gives

\[
|\mathbf p_N|\ge Q^{-C'}
\]

and hence D4.

The other chart \(\Gamma_{G,2}\ne0\) is handled symmetrically with

\[
w=\frac{\Gamma_G}{\Gamma_{G,2}}.
\]

So the D4 nonlinear row theorem can be certified by two local Nullstellensatz identities.

---

# 3. Two-row resultant version

For \(A_7,A_9\) alone, the common-zero condition is

\[
p_7(Y,r,z)=0,
\qquad
p_9(Y,r,z)=0.
\]

Compute

\[
R_{79}(Y,r):=\operatorname{Res}_z(p_7,p_9).
\]

If

\[
R_{79}(Y,r)
\]

has no zeros for

\[
r\in[r_-,r_+],
\]

then \(A_7,A_9\) observe all anisotropy directions.

A useful quantitative sufficient condition is:

\[
|R_{79}(Y,r)|\ge Q^{-C}
\quad\text{for all admissible }r.
\]

Then standard resultant bounds imply

\[
|p_7|+|p_9|\ge Q^{-C'}
\]

on the admissible compact region, assuming coefficient heights and degrees are polynomially controlled.

If \(R_{79}\) has admissible roots, then \(A_7,A_9\) fail at those \(r\)-levels and we must add \(A_{11}\) or use escape.

---

# 4. Three-row kill of common roots

If \(A_7,A_9\) have a common root set, add \(p_{11}\).

The ideal test becomes:

\[
1\in (p_7,p_9,p_{11})
\]

on the admissible algebra.

Equivalently, after computing common roots of \(p_7,p_9\), verify

\[
p_{11}\ne0
\]

at each root.

This is finite because in the projective anisotropy variable \(z\), the common roots of two nontrivial polynomials are finite after resultant reduction.

If \(p_{11}\) vanishes at all those roots, add \(p_{13}\).

Thus the finite row audit has an exact stopping rule:

\[
\boxed{
\text{add rows until no common admissible projective anisotropy root remains.}
}
\]

If no finite row in the known tower kills the common roots, then the row architecture is genuinely blind to some anisotropy direction.

---

# 5. Expected easy certificate for \(A_7,A_9\)

In the expected linear case,

\[
p_7=-\frac{c_7}{288},
\]

\[
p_9=-\frac{c_9}{288}z
\]

in the \(\Gamma_G\ne0\) chart.

Then

\[
p_7
\]

is already nonzero if

\[
c_7\ne0.
\]

So there is no common zero in this chart.

In the other chart,

\[
w=\Gamma_G/\Gamma_{G,2},
\]

one gets

\[
p_7=-\frac{c_7}{288}w,
\]

\[
p_9=-\frac{c_9}{288}.
\]

Then \(p_9\) is nonzero if

\[
c_9\ne0.
\]

Thus the two-chart certificate is trivial:

\[
\boxed{
c_7c_9\ne0.
}
\]

This is why the displayed row asymptotics are so powerful. If they are literal principal symbols, D4 is solved.

---

# 6. Handling same-order nonlinear terms

Suppose instead

\[
p_7(z,r)=a_0+a_1z+r(a_2+a_3z+a_4z^2)+\cdots,
\]

\[
p_9(z,r)=b_0+b_1z+r(b_2+b_3z+b_4z^2)+\cdots.
\]

The bad set is finite algebraic. The first audit is:

\[
R_{79}(r)=\operatorname{Res}_z(p_7,p_9).
\]

If

\[
R_{79}(r)\not\equiv0,
\]

then common roots occur only at roots of \(R_{79}\).

If \(R_{79}\equiv0\), the two rows share a common factor in \(z\). Then \(A_7,A_9\) are not enough; add rows.

If \(R_{79}\) has roots in \([r_-,r_+]\), check \(p_{11}\) at those roots.

So same-order nonlinear terms do not make D4 conceptual. They only require a finite resultant/subresultant audit.

---

# 7. Escape from isolated resultant roots

If a common-root obstruction occurs only at isolated levels

\[
r=r_0(Y),
\]

then existential sampling gives another escape route.

Let

\[
S(m)=R_{79}(Y(m),r(m)).
\]

If a D4 sample lies near

\[
S=0,
\]

but

\[
|S'|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

or higher derivative is live, then move slightly to a point where

\[
|S|\ge Q^{-C'}
\]

while

\[
q_0,\quad u,\quad |\Gamma|
\]

remain live. This is the same mechanism as wall escape.

Thus even if \(A_7,A_9\) fail on a secondary hypersurface, existential sampling may avoid it.

Only if Gate 2 mass is locked to a component where all relevant resultants vanish identically does one need more rows or a new invariant.

---

# 8. Row-polynomial D4 theorem

The robust theorem is:

## D4 Row-Polynomial Observability Theorem

Let

\[
\mathbf A_N=q_0u^3\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)+\mathbf E_N.
\]

Assume:

1. Projective observability:

\[
|\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)|\ge Q^{-C_P}|\Gamma|
\]

on the D4 admissible set.

2. Residual control:

\[
|\mathbf E_N|\le
\frac12 |q_0||u|^3Q^{-C_P}|\Gamma|.
\]

Then

\[
|\mathbf A_N|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

at every D4 sample.

With row mobility,

\[
\int_I|\mathbf A_N|^2\ge Q^{-C'}|I|.
\]

Projective observability can be proved by either:

- principal-symbol determinant \(c_7c_9\ne0\);
- resultant nonvanishing for \(p_7,p_9\);
- adding rows and proving a Nullstellensatz identity;
- secondary escape from resultant sublevels.

---

# 9. Finite audit protocol

To execute D4:

1. Compute principal row polynomials

\[
p_7,p_9.
\]

2. If they are linear diagonal, verify

\[
c_7c_9\ne0
\]

and stop.

3. If nonlinear, compute

\[
R_{79}=\operatorname{Res}_z(p_7,p_9).
\]

4. If \(R_{79}\) has no admissible root, stop.

5. If roots exist, add \(p_{11}\) and test common zeros.

6. If necessary add \(p_{13}\).

7. If finite rows still share a common anisotropy root, identify the common factor or row-blind anisotropy direction.

8. Either add a different row family or state the row architecture defect.

---

# 10. Conclusion

D4 is now finite algebra plus error bounds.

The expected close is:

\[
\operatorname{psym}_{D4}(A_7)=(-c_7/288,0),
\]

\[
\operatorname{psym}_{D4}(A_9)=(0,-c_9/288),
\]

with

\[
c_7c_9\ne0.
\]

If confirmed, all nonlinear/resultant machinery is unnecessary.

If not, the finite projective row-polynomial audit gives the next exact steps.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 11:34:32)

_Thought for 9s_

## Continuing attack — force D4 linearity by row degree / anisotropy order

The D4 row problem is now:

\[
\mathbf A_N=q_0u^3\mathbf P_N(Y,\Gamma)+\mathbf E_N,
\qquad
\Gamma=(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2}).
\]

If the row-polynomial principal part is linear and triangular,

\[
A_7\leadsto q_0u^3\Gamma_G,
\qquad
A_9\leadsto q_0u^3\Gamma_{G,2},
\]

then D4 closes. The current downstream route already places this after Gate 2 and Wall-Escape Sampling. 

The next attack is to show that same-order nonlinear anisotropy terms like

\[
q_0u^3\Gamma^2
\]

should be impossible in \(A_7,A_9\) at the principal row-symbol level.

---

# 1. Assign row degree

The observed tower is

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\Gamma_G,
\]

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\Gamma_{G,2},
\]

with

\[
H\sim q_0u^3.
\]

This suggests a grading:

\[
\deg(H)=0,
\]

\[
\deg(\Gamma_G)=7,
\]

\[
\deg(\Gamma_{G,2})=9,
\]

or, after stripping the common \(H\)-factor,

\[
\deg(\Gamma_G)=1,
\qquad
\deg(\Gamma_{G,2})=2.
\]

Then \(A_7\) is the first row where \(\Gamma_G\) can appear, and \(A_9\) is the first row where \(\Gamma_{G,2}\) can appear.

A nonlinear term

\[
H\Gamma_G^2
\]

would have anisotropy degree \(2\), so it should first appear at the same level as \(\Gamma_{G,2}\), not in \(A_7\). A term

\[
H\Gamma_G\Gamma_{G,2}
\]

has anisotropy degree \(3\), so it should first appear later than \(A_9\).

Thus the associated graded row structure should be:

\[
\operatorname{gr}(A_7/H)=c_7\Gamma_G,
\]

\[
\operatorname{gr}(A_9/H)=c_9\Gamma_{G,2}
+
\text{possible lower-channel multiple of }\Gamma_G.
\]

No quadratic anisotropy term should be same-order in \(A_7\) or \(A_9\).

---

# 2. Filtered linearity theorem

The theorem to prove is:

## Filtered Linearity of Low Rows

In the associated graded algebra of the downstream row tower,

\[
\operatorname{gr}_{\le 1}(A_7/H)
=
c_7\Gamma_G,
\]

and

\[
\operatorname{gr}_{\le 2}(A_9/H)
=
b_9\Gamma_G+c_9\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Moreover,

\[
c_7c_9\ne0.
\]

All terms nonlinear in

\[
(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2})
\]

have strictly higher filtration and are excluded from the D4 principal symbol for \(A_7,A_9\).

If this holds, then same-order nonlinear anisotropy terms are absent, and D4 reduces to the \(2\times2\) determinant

\[
\Delta_{7,9}
=
c_7c_9
\]

after lower-channel row elimination.

---

# 3. Consequence for D4

Under filtered linearity, write

\[
A_7
=
H(c_7\Gamma_G)+E_7,
\]

\[
A_9
=
H(b_9\Gamma_G+c_9\Gamma_{G,2})+E_9.
\]

Since

\[
H=-\frac{q_0u^3}{288}+E_H,
\]

we get

\[
A_7
=
-\frac{c_7}{288}q_0u^3\Gamma_G+\widetilde E_7,
\]

\[
A_9
=
-\frac{1}{288}q_0u^3(b_9\Gamma_G+c_9\Gamma_{G,2})
+\widetilde E_9.
\]

Perform row elimination:

\[
\widetilde A_9
=
A_9-\frac{b_9}{c_7}A_7.
\]

Then

\[
\widetilde A_9
=
-\frac{c_9}{288}q_0u^3\Gamma_{G,2}
+
\widetilde E_9'.
\]

So the triangularized rows are

\[
A_7
=
-\frac{c_7}{288}q_0u^3\Gamma_G+\widetilde E_7,
\]

\[
\widetilde A_9
=
-\frac{c_9}{288}q_0u^3\Gamma_{G,2}
+
\widetilde E_9'.
\]

If

\[
|\widetilde E_7|+|\widetilde E_9'|
\le
\frac12
|q_0||u|^3
\min(|c_7|,|c_9|)
|\Gamma|/288,
\]

then

\[
\left(|A_7|^2+|\widetilde A_9|^2\right)^{1/2}
\ge
Q^{-C}
|q_0||u|^3|\Gamma|.
\]

At a D4 sample this gives the desired row lower bound.

---

# 4. Attack on the error terms

Now the error terms are precisely those of higher filtration.

Let the higher-filtration gap be at least one small packet factor \(\kappa\), where

\[
\kappa\le Q^{-C_\kappa}.
\]

Then

\[
|\widetilde E|
\le
Q^{C_E}\kappa |q_0||u|^3|\Gamma|.
\]

Choose the row depth / packet normalization so that

\[
Q^{C_E}\kappa
\le
\frac12Q^{-C_L}.
\]

Then half-leading correction control follows automatically.

This is a strong route: instead of estimating every correction separately, prove a **filtration gap**.

The needed theorem is:

## Low-Row Filtration Gap

All non-principal terms in \(A_7,A_9\) after extracting \(H\Gamma_G,H\Gamma_{G,2}\) carry at least one additional small normalized factor \(\kappa\le Q^{-C}\), or are canonical \(E_2\)/tail terms bounded by the same scale.

Then D4 correction control follows.

---

# 5. What to check in actual formulas

For each term in \(A_7,A_9\), after dividing by \(H\), classify it as:

\[
\Gamma_G,
\]

\[
\Gamma_{G,2},
\]

\[
\Gamma_G^2,\quad
\Gamma_G\Gamma_{G,2},\quad
\Gamma_{G,2}^2,
\]

\[
\text{derivative anisotropy},
\]

\[
\text{tail/gauge/projection error}.
\]

Then verify:

1. in \(A_7/H\), the only filtration-1 term is \(c_7\Gamma_G\);

2. in \(A_9/H\), the first new filtration-2 term is \(c_9\Gamma_{G,2}\);

3. any \(A_9/H\) term proportional to \(\Gamma_G\) is removable by \(A_7\);

4. nonlinear anisotropy terms have higher filtration;

5. all higher-filtration terms have polynomially small coefficient at D4 samples.

This is a finite associated-graded check.

---

# 6. If nonlinear terms are not higher filtration

Suppose the audit finds a same-filtration nonlinear term, for example

\[
A_9/H
=
c_9\Gamma_{G,2}
+
d\,\Gamma_G^2
+
\cdots.
\]

Then D4 becomes a nonlinear row-polynomial theorem.

But this may still be manageable because, after triangularization,

\[
\widetilde A_9/H
=
c_9\Gamma_{G,2}
+
d\,\Gamma_G^2
+
\cdots.
\]

If the first row controls \(\Gamma_G\), then the second row needs to control the remaining direction. A cancellation

\[
c_9\Gamma_{G,2}+d\Gamma_G^2\approx0
]

does not kill row conversion unless \(A_7\) is also small. But \(A_7\) is proportional to \(\Gamma_G\). If \(\Gamma_G\) is large, \(A_7\) already works. If \(\Gamma_G\) is small, then \(\Gamma_G^2\) is negligible and \(\widetilde A_9\) controls \(\Gamma_{G,2}\).

This gives a useful nonlinear triangular argument.

---

# 7. Nonlinear triangular lemma

Assume

\[
A_7/H=c_7\Gamma_G+E_7,
\]

\[
\widetilde A_9/H
=
c_9\Gamma_{G,2}
+
P(\Gamma_G)
+
E_9,
\]

where \(P\) has order at least \(2\) in \(\Gamma_G\).

Then

\[
|A_7/H|+|\widetilde A_9/H|
\ge
Q^{-C}\left(|\Gamma_G|+|\Gamma_{G,2}|\right)
\]

provided errors are half-leading.

Proof split:

- If

\[
|\Gamma_G|\ge \epsilon |\Gamma|,
\]

then \(A_7\) controls \(\Gamma\).

- If

\[
|\Gamma_G|<\epsilon |\Gamma|,
\]

then

\[
|\Gamma_{G,2}|\asymp |\Gamma|,
\]

and

\[
|P(\Gamma_G)|\le C\epsilon^2|\Gamma|^2.
\]

For controlled \(|\Gamma|\) or sufficiently small \(\epsilon\), \(P\) cannot cancel the linear \(c_9\Gamma_{G,2}\) term.

Thus even certain nonlinear terms are harmless.

This is stronger than requiring nonlinear terms to be absent.

---

# 8. Resulting D4 proof route

D4 can be closed by one of two finite structures.

## Route 1 — linear associated-graded triangularity

\[
A_7/H=c_7\Gamma_G+\text{higher},
\]

\[
A_9/H=b_9\Gamma_G+c_9\Gamma_{G,2}+\text{higher},
\]

with \(c_7c_9\ne0\).

## Route 2 — nonlinear triangularity

\[
A_7/H=c_7\Gamma_G+\text{higher},
\]

\[
\widetilde A_9/H
=
c_9\Gamma_{G,2}
+
P(\Gamma_G)
+
\text{higher},
\]

where \(P\) has no linear \(\Gamma_{G,2}\)-cancelling factor and is controlled when \(A_7\) is small.

Either route gives

\[
|(A_7,A_9)|\ge Q^{-C}|q_0||u|^3|\Gamma|.
\]

Thus D4 closes.

---

# 9. Concrete next proof obligation

The next formula audit should not just compute \(\det L\). It should compute the triangularized normal form:

\[
A_7/H
\]

and

\[
A_9/H-\frac{[\Gamma_G](A_9/H)}{[\Gamma_G](A_7/H)}A_7/H.
\]

Then inspect the second expression:

\[
\widetilde A_9/H.
\]

Need to show its principal dependence on \(\Gamma_{G,2}\) has nonzero coefficient.

That is the exact finite test.

---

# 10. Attack conclusion

D4 is likely closable under a triangular row hierarchy:

\[
A_7\text{ controls }\Gamma_G,
\qquad
A_9\text{ controls }\Gamma_{G,2}\text{ modulo }A_7.
\]

Even if nonlinear terms appear, they are harmless if they depend only on already-controlled lower channels or are higher filtration.

So the D4 finite audit should target the triangularized row normal form, not merely raw determinant expansion.

## Continuing attack — prove nonlinear triangular D4 from \(A_7\)-control

I will now make the nonlinear-triangular argument proof-level. The goal is to avoid requiring that \(A_9/H\) be purely linear in \(\Gamma_{G,2}\). It is enough that any nonlinear terms depend only on the lower channel already controlled by \(A_7\), or are perturbative on the complementary region.

The current row architecture is

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\Gamma_G,\qquad
A_9^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\Gamma_{G,2},
\qquad
H\sim q_0u^3,
\]

and D4 is the matrix row-conversion layer after Gate 2 and Wall-Escape Sampling. 

---

# 1. Normalize by \(H\)

On the non-wall D4 sample, \(H\) is nonzero to polynomial scale:

\[
H=-\frac{q_0u^3}{288}+\mathcal R_H,
\]

with

\[
|q_0|\ge Q^{-C_q},\qquad |u|\ge Q^{-C_u}.
\]

Assume the \(H\)-error is half-leading:

\[
|\mathcal R_H|
\le c|q_0||u|^3.
\]

Then dividing by \(H\) is stable up to polynomial constants. Define normalized rows

\[
B_7:=\frac{A_7^{\mathfrak f}}{H},
\qquad
B_9:=\frac{A_9^{\mathfrak f}}{H}.
\]

The expected structure is

\[
B_7=c_7\Gamma_G+E_7,
\]

\[
B_9=b_9\Gamma_G+c_9\Gamma_{G,2}+P(\Gamma_G)+E_9,
\]

where \(P\) is at least quadratic in \(\Gamma_G\), and

\[
c_7c_9\ne0.
\]

Subtract the lower-channel part:

\[
\widetilde B_9
:=
B_9-\frac{b_9}{c_7}B_7.
\]

Then

\[
\widetilde B_9
=
c_9\Gamma_{G,2}+P(\Gamma_G)+\widetilde E_9.
\]

So the row vector is effectively

\[
(B_7,\widetilde B_9)
=
(c_7\Gamma_G,\ c_9\Gamma_{G,2}+P(\Gamma_G))
+
\text{controlled errors}.
\]

---

# 2. Nonlinear triangular lemma

## Lemma

Let

\[
X=\Gamma_G,\qquad Y=\Gamma_{G,2},
\qquad |\Gamma|=(X^2+Y^2)^{1/2}.
\]

Assume

\[
B_7=c_7X+E_7,
\]

\[
\widetilde B_9=c_9Y+P(X)+E_9,
\]

with

\[
|c_7|,|c_9|\ge Q^{-C_c}.
\]

Assume \(P\) satisfies the lower-channel quadratic bound

\[
|P(X)|\le Q^{C_P}|X|^2.
\]

Assume errors satisfy

\[
|(E_7,E_9)|\le \epsilon_0 Q^{-C_c}|\Gamma|
\]

for a sufficiently small absolute \(\epsilon_0\).

Assume a polynomial anisotropy upper bound

\[
|\Gamma|\le Q^{C_\Gamma}.
\]

Then

\[
\boxed{
|B_7|+|\widetilde B_9|
\ge Q^{-C}|\Gamma|
}
\]

provided we either:

1. sample in a controlled anisotropy layer with \(Q^{C_P}|\Gamma|\le cQ^{-C_c}\), or  
2. use the two-case lower-channel split below.

---

# 3. Two-case proof

Choose a threshold

\[
\theta:=Q^{-K}
\]

with \(K\) to be fixed.

## Case 1: lower channel visible

Assume

\[
|X|\ge \theta |\Gamma|.
\]

Then

\[
|B_7|
\ge
|c_7||X|-|E_7|
\ge
Q^{-C_c}\theta|\Gamma|-\epsilon_0Q^{-C_c}|\Gamma|.
\]

Choose

\[
\epsilon_0\le \frac12\theta.
\]

Then

\[
|B_7|\ge \frac12Q^{-C_c}\theta|\Gamma|.
\]

Thus

\[
|B_7|+|\widetilde B_9|\ge Q^{-C}|\Gamma|.
\]

So whenever \(\Gamma_G\) is not too small, \(A_7\) alone controls the anisotropy.

## Case 2: lower channel small

Assume

\[
|X|<\theta |\Gamma|.
\]

Then

\[
|Y|
\ge (1-\theta^2)^{1/2}|\Gamma|
\ge \frac12|\Gamma|
\]

for small \(\theta\).

The nonlinear lower-channel term satisfies

\[
|P(X)|
\le
Q^{C_P}|X|^2
\le
Q^{C_P}\theta^2|\Gamma|^2.
\]

Using

\[
|\Gamma|\le Q^{C_\Gamma},
\]

we get

\[
|P(X)|
\le
Q^{C_P+C_\Gamma}\theta^2|\Gamma|.
\]

Choose

\[
\theta=Q^{-K}
\]

with

\[
2K>C_P+C_\Gamma+C_c+10.
\]

Then

\[
|P(X)|\le \frac14Q^{-C_c}|\Gamma|.
\]

Also choose the error bound so that

\[
|E_9|\le \frac14Q^{-C_c}|\Gamma|.
\]

Then

\[
|\widetilde B_9|
\ge
|c_9||Y|-|P(X)|-|E_9|
\ge
\frac12Q^{-C_c}|\Gamma|-\frac14Q^{-C_c}|\Gamma|-\frac14Q^{-C_c}|\Gamma|.
\]

With slightly stronger constants, this gives

\[
|\widetilde B_9|\ge cQ^{-C}|\Gamma|.
\]

Thus the second row controls anisotropy when the first channel is small.

---

# 4. Consequence for original rows

Since

\[
A_7=HB_7,
\qquad
\widetilde A_9=H\widetilde B_9,
\]

we get

\[
|(A_7,\widetilde A_9)|
\ge
|H|Q^{-C}|\Gamma|.
\]

With

\[
|H|\ge c|q_0||u|^3,
\]

this gives

\[
|(A_7,\widetilde A_9)|
\ge
Q^{-C}|q_0||u|^3|\Gamma|.
\]

At a D4 sample,

\[
|q_0|\ge Q^{-C_q},
\]

\[
|u|^2|\Gamma|^2\ge Q^{-A_2},
\]

and either

\[
|u|\ge Q^{-C_u}
\]

or

\[
|\Gamma|\le Q^{C_\Gamma}
\]

so that \(|u|\) is recovered from Gate 2 mass. Hence

\[
|(A_7,\widetilde A_9)|\ge Q^{-C'}.
\]

Because the row operation

\[
\widetilde A_9=A_9-\frac{b_9}{c_7}A_7
\]

has polynomially bounded coefficient, this implies

\[
|(A_7,A_9)|\ge Q^{-C''}.
\]

Thus D4 closes.

---

# 5. What this proves conceptually

This nonlinear triangular lemma shows that same-order terms like

\[
P(\Gamma_G)=d\Gamma_G^2+\cdots
\]

inside the second row are not fatal.

Reason:

- If \(\Gamma_G\) is large, \(A_7\) is already large.
- If \(\Gamma_G\) is small, then \(P(\Gamma_G)\) is too small to cancel the linear \(\Gamma_{G,2}\) term in \(A_9\).

Therefore, the only dangerous nonlinear terms are those containing a **linear factor in \(\Gamma_{G,2}\)** with coefficients depending on \(\Gamma_G\) strongly enough to cancel \(c_9\Gamma_{G,2}\), such as

\[
\Gamma_G\Gamma_{G,2}
\]

when \(\Gamma_G\) is not small.

But if \(\Gamma_G\) is not small, \(A_7\) again controls. So even mixed terms are often harmless under the two-case split.

---

# 6. More general triangular polynomial theorem

The argument extends to

\[
\widetilde B_9
=
c_9Y+
P(X,Y),
\]

provided every monomial of \(P\) either:

1. contains \(X\) as a factor, or  
2. is at least quadratic in \(Y\) but controlled in a small-anisotropy layer.

For terms with an \(X\)-factor:

\[
P(X,Y)=X\,R(X,Y).
\]

If \(|X|\ge\theta|\Gamma|\), \(A_7\) controls.

If \(|X|<\theta|\Gamma|\), then

\[
|P(X,Y)|\le \theta|\Gamma|\,|R(X,Y)|.
\]

With polynomial upper bounds and sufficiently small \(\theta\), this is perturbative compared with \(c_9Y\).

Thus any nonlinear term containing the already-controlled channel \(X=\Gamma_G\) is harmless.

The truly dangerous terms in \(\widetilde B_9\) are pure powers of \(Y\):

\[
Y^2,\quad Y^3,\ldots
\]

because they can cancel

\[
c_9Y
\]

when \(Y\) is not small.

But those are higher powers of the new channel. They are harmless in a controlled-anisotropy layer

\[
|Y|\le \gamma_+\ll1.
\]

If \(Y\) is large, use anisotropy first-entry or nonlinear row-polynomial observability.

---

# 7. Refined D4 sufficient conditions

D4 closes if, after triangularization,

\[
B_7=c_7X+E_7,
\]

\[
\widetilde B_9=c_9Y+P_X(X,Y)+P_Y(Y)+E_9,
\]

where:

\[
P_X(X,Y)\in (X)
\]

and

\[
P_Y(Y)=O(Y^2).
\]

Then D4 closes on the controlled-anisotropy region

\[
|\Gamma|\le \gamma_+
\]

by choosing \(\gamma_+\) sufficiently small.

For the large-anisotropy region, use first-entry to reduce to \(|\Gamma|=\gamma_+\), or use row-polynomial observability.

Thus D4 is robust against many nonlinear corrections.

---

# 8. Updated row audit

The row audit should no longer ask merely:

\[
\text{Are nonlinear terms present?}
\]

It should ask:

1. After triangularizing \(A_9\) by \(A_7\), do nonlinear terms contain the already-controlled channel \(\Gamma_G\)?
2. Are there pure powers of the new channel \(\Gamma_{G,2}\)?
3. If pure powers of \(\Gamma_{G,2}\) exist, can sampling be restricted to \(|\Gamma|\le \gamma_+\)?
4. If not, do additional rows remove the pure-\(Y\) nonlinear roots?

This is a more precise and more forgiving audit.

---

# 9. Attack conclusion

D4 does **not** fail merely because nonlinear anisotropy terms appear.

It closes under a triangular nonlinear structure:

\[
A_7/H=c_7\Gamma_G+\cdots,
\]

\[
A_9/H=b_9\Gamma_G+c_9\Gamma_{G,2}
+
\text{terms divisible by }\Gamma_G
+
O(\Gamma_{G,2}^2)
+
\cdots.
\]

The proof is a simple two-case split:

- if \(\Gamma_G\) is large, \(A_7\) is large;
- if \(\Gamma_G\) is small, nonlinear terms involving \(\Gamma_G\) are perturbative and \(A_9\) controls \(\Gamma_{G,2}\).

Only pure high powers of \(\Gamma_{G,2}\) in the second row require controlled-anisotropy sampling or additional rows.

This materially weakens the D4 audit burden.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 11:36:26)

_Thought for a few seconds_

## Continuing attack — remove pure \(\Gamma_{G,2}\)-nonlinear obstruction by first-entry or row differentiation

The remaining D4 risk after nonlinear triangularization is narrow:

\[
\widetilde B_9
=
c_9Y+P_X(X,Y)+P_Y(Y)+E_9,
\]

where

\[
X=\Gamma_G,\qquad Y=\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Terms in

\[
P_X(X,Y)\in (X)
\]

are harmless by the two-case split: if \(X\) is large, \(A_7\) controls; if \(X\) is small, \(P_X\) is perturbative.

The only genuinely dangerous terms are pure powers

\[
P_Y(Y)=d_2Y^2+d_3Y^3+\cdots,
\]

because

\[
c_9Y+P_Y(Y)
\]

can vanish for nonzero \(Y\). This is the last local D4 obstruction. The current downstream plan is Gate 2 plus Wall-Escape Sampling plus D4 matrix row conversion, so this is exactly where the row-conversion layer must be made robust. 

---

# 1. Factor the pure-\(Y\) obstruction

Write

\[
c_9Y+P_Y(Y)
=
Y\,R_Y(Y),
\]

where

\[
R_Y(Y)=c_9+d_2Y+d_3Y^2+\cdots.
\]

The second row controls \(Y\) unless

\[
R_Y(Y)\approx0.
\]

Thus the pure-\(Y\) obstruction is not all large \(Y\). It is the finite root set of the scalar polynomial

\[
R_Y(Y)=0.
\]

So after \(A_7\) has controlled \(X\), the residual bad set is

\[
\boxed{
X\approx0,\qquad R_Y(Y)\approx0,\qquad Y\ne0.
}
\]

This is a one-dimensional finite algebraic obstruction, not a broad analytic one.

---

# 2. Root-avoidance by first-entry in \(Y\)

Let the roots of \(R_Y\) be

\[
\zeta_1,\ldots,\zeta_s.
\]

If \(Y\) is near a root \(\zeta_j\), but \(Y'\) is polynomially non-small, then one can move slightly to make

\[
|R_Y(Y)|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

while preserving \(q_0,u,\Gamma\) by the same sampling/mobility logic used for the \(q_0\)-wall.

This gives a **secondary first-exit theorem**:

## Pure-\(Y\) Root Escape

Assume

\[
|R_Y(Y(m_0))|\le Q^{-C_0},
\qquad
|Y'(m_0)|\ge Q^{-C_Y}.
\]

Assume

\[
|Y''|\le Q^{C}
\]

locally. Then there exists

\[
m_1,\qquad |m_1-m_0|\le Q^{-C'}
\]

such that

\[
|R_Y(Y(m_1))|\ge Q^{-C''},
\]

while \(q_0,u,|\Gamma|\) remain polynomially live.

Then the triangularized row

\[
\widetilde B_9=Y R_Y(Y)+\text{controlled terms}
\]

is non-small.

So pure-\(Y\) cancellation can be escaped unless \(Y\) is trapped near a root over an interval.

---

# 3. If \(Y\) is trapped near a root over an interval

Suppose

\[
Y(m)\approx \zeta
\]

on an interval, with

\[
X=\Gamma_G\approx0.
\]

Then

\[
\Gamma_G\approx0,
\qquad
\Gamma_{G,2}\approx \zeta.
\]

If \(\zeta\ne0\), Gate 2 is live through \(Y\), but the row pair \(A_7,A_9\) may vanish because the polynomial row factor \(R_Y(\zeta)=0\).

This is exactly when a third row should resolve the ambiguity.

Let

\[
A_{11}/H
\]

after triangularization have residual dependence

\[
B_{11}
=
c_{11,1}X+c_{11,2}Y+Q_{11}(X,Y).
\]

On the trapped set

\[
X=0,\qquad Y=\zeta,
\]

we need

\[
B_{11}(0,\zeta)\ne0.
\]

Thus the finite audit becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{For every nonzero root }\zeta\text{ of }R_Y,\text{ check }B_{11}(0,\zeta)\ne0.
}
\]

If true, adding \(A_{11}\) removes the pure-\(Y\) secondary wall.

If false, check \(A_{13}\), etc.

---

# 4. Row-polynomial root-killing hierarchy

This gives a finite hierarchy:

1. \(A_7\) controls \(X=\Gamma_G\).
2. After eliminating \(X\), \(A_9\) controls \(Y=\Gamma_{G,2}\) except at finite roots of \(R_Y(Y)\).
3. \(A_{11}\) is checked at those roots.
4. If needed, \(A_{13}\), etc.

Formally, define triangularized row polynomials:

\[
P_7(X,Y)=X,
\]

\[
P_9(X,Y)=Y R_Y(Y)+X\cdot S_9(X,Y),
\]

\[
P_{11}(X,Y),\quad P_{13}(X,Y),\ldots.
\]

The common-root condition is

\[
P_7=P_9=P_{11}=\cdots=0.
\]

Since \(P_7=0\) implies \(X=0\), the remaining condition is finite:

\[
Y R_Y(Y)=0,
\]

plus

\[
P_{11}(0,Y)=0,\quad P_{13}(0,Y)=0,\ldots.
\]

Gate 2 excludes \(Y=0\) if \(X=0\), because then \(\Gamma=0\). Thus only nonzero roots of \(R_Y\) matter.

So the finite certificate is:

\[
\boxed{
\gcd\left(YR_Y(Y),\,P_{11}(0,Y),\,P_{13}(0,Y),\ldots\right)=Y.
}
\]

Equivalently, after removing the allowed zero \(Y=0\), the row polynomials have no common root.

That is a one-variable gcd computation.

---

# 5. Nullstellensatz certificate in the triangular chart

The above can be written as a simple ideal certificate.

In the quotient by \(X\), require

\[
Y^N\in (P_9(0,Y),P_{11}(0,Y),P_{13}(0,Y),\ldots)
\]

or, better, since \(Y\ne0\) on the Gate 2-live branch, require

\[
1\in (R_Y(Y),P_{11}(0,Y),P_{13}(0,Y),\ldots)
\]

after localizing at \(Y\).

A quantitative certificate is:

\[
1
=
B_9(Y)R_Y(Y)
+
B_{11}(Y)P_{11}(0,Y)
+
B_{13}(Y)P_{13}(0,Y)
+\cdots
\]

with polynomially bounded coefficients on the admissible \(Y\)-range.

Then

\[
|R_Y(Y)|+|P_{11}(0,Y)|+|P_{13}(0,Y)|+\cdots
\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

Multiplying back by \(|Y|\) gives row lower bound.

---

# 6. If \(R_Y\) has no admissible nonzero roots

Then no extra rows are needed.

If the roots of \(R_Y\) are all outside the admissible anisotropy range

\[
Y\in [Q^{-C_-},Q^{C_+}]
\]

or complex/non-real while \(Y\) is real, then \(A_7,A_9\) alone close D4.

This is likely if \(R_Y(Y)=c_9+dY+\cdots\) with small higher coefficients.

So the pure-\(Y\) audit starts with:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Compute roots of }R_Y(Y).
}
\]

If none are admissible, stop.

---

# 7. If \(Y\) is complex-valued

If rows and anisotropy are complex, replace real roots by projective complex roots.

Then \(Y\) is a complex coordinate. The same gcd/resultant logic works in \(\mathbb C[Y]\). The Gate 2 norm condition gives

\[
|Y|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

on the \(X\approx0\) branch.

The finite certificate is still:

\[
1\in (R_Y(Y),P_{11}(0,Y),P_{13}(0,Y),\ldots)
\]

on the annulus

\[
Q^{-C_-}\le |Y|\le Q^{C_+}.
\]

Quantitative lower bounds follow from polynomial coefficient bounds and absence of common roots in the annulus.

---

# 8. Consequence for D4 audit

The D4 audit is now split into three increasingly narrow checks:

## Check A — principal triangularity

\[
A_7/H=c_7X+\cdots,
\]

\[
A_9/H=b_9X+c_9Y+\cdots,
\]

with

\[
c_7c_9\ne0.
\]

If no pure \(Y^2,Y^3,\ldots\) terms occur, D4 is closed.

## Check B — pure-\(Y\) scalar factor

If pure \(Y\)-nonlinear terms occur, compute

\[
R_Y(Y)
\]

where

\[
\widetilde A_9/H=Y R_Y(Y)+X(\cdots).
\]

If \(R_Y\) has no admissible nonzero roots, D4 is closed.

## Check C — root killing by extra rows

For each admissible nonzero root \(\zeta\) of \(R_Y\), check whether

\[
A_{11}/H
\]

or higher rows are nonzero at

\[
X=0,\quad Y=\zeta.
\]

If yes for all roots, D4 closes with extended rows.

---

# 9. Attack conclusion

Even in the hardest nonlinear triangular case, D4 reduces to a **one-variable polynomial root audit** after \(A_7\) controls \(\Gamma_G\).

The dangerous branch is:

\[
\Gamma_G=0,\qquad \Gamma_{G,2}=Y\ne0,
\]

and

\[
\widetilde A_9/H=Y R_Y(Y)=0.
\]

Thus the exact finite test is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Do higher rows kill every nonzero admissible root of }R_Y?
}
\]

If yes, D4 closes.

This is much simpler than a full two-variable row-polynomial observability problem.

## Continuing attack — reduce nonlinear D4 to a one-variable Euclidean certificate

We have narrowed the worst D4 case to the triangularized chart

\[
X=\Gamma_G,\qquad Y=\Gamma_{G,2},
\]

with

\[
A_7/H=c_7X+\cdots,
\]

and after eliminating the \(X\)-channel from \(A_9\),

\[
\widetilde A_9/H
=
Y R_Y(Y)+X(\cdots)+E_9.
\]

The dangerous branch is now extremely specific:

\[
X\approx0,\qquad Y\ne0,\qquad R_Y(Y)\approx0.
\]

So D4 reduces to a one-variable polynomial root-killing problem. This is a major simplification of the row-polynomial observability problem described in the downstream update. 

---

# 1. Triangularized row system

Normalize by \(H\), or equivalently by \(q_0u^3\) up to the known factor

\[
H\sim -\frac{q_0u^3}{288}.
\]

After row elimination, assume the row family has the form

\[
B_7
=
c_7X+E_7,
\]

\[
\widetilde B_9
=
Y R_9(Y)+X S_9(X,Y)+E_9,
\]

\[
\widetilde B_{11}
=
P_{11}(X,Y)+E_{11},
\]

\[
\widetilde B_{13}
=
P_{13}(X,Y)+E_{13},
\]

where

\[
X=\Gamma_G,\qquad Y=\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Gate 2 gives

\[
|X|^2+|Y|^2=|\Gamma|^2
\]

weighted by \(|u|^2\). At a D4 sample we have

\[
|u|^2(X^2+Y^2)\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

If \(X\) is polynomially non-small, then \(B_7\) controls the row lower bound.

Thus only the branch

\[
|X|\ll |Y|
\]

needs attention.

On that branch,

\[
|Y|\asymp |\Gamma|.
\]

So the remaining task is to show

\[
Y R_9(Y),\quad P_{11}(0,Y),\quad P_{13}(0,Y),\ldots
\]

do not all vanish for nonzero admissible \(Y\).

---

# 2. Localize away from \(Y=0\)

The root \(Y=0\) is harmless in this branch.

If

\[
X=0,\qquad Y=0,
\]

then

\[
\Gamma=0,
\]

so Gate 2 mass is zero.

Therefore we may localize at \(Y\ne0\). Algebraically, work in

\[
\mathbb C[Y,Y^{-1}]
\]

or restrict analytically to the annulus

\[
Q^{-C_-}\le |Y|\le Q^{C_+}.
\]

After localizing by \(Y\), the equation

\[
Y R_9(Y)=0
\]

is equivalent to

\[
R_9(Y)=0.
\]

So the dangerous branch is:

\[
R_9(Y)=0,\qquad P_{11}(0,Y)=0,\qquad P_{13}(0,Y)=0,\ldots
\]

with

\[
Y\ne0.
\]

---

# 3. Euclidean certificate

A finite proof of D4 in this chart is an identity

\[
1
=
U_9(Y)R_9(Y)
+
U_{11}(Y)P_{11}(0,Y)
+
U_{13}(Y)P_{13}(0,Y)
+\cdots
\]

in the localized ring \(\mathbb C[Y,Y^{-1}]\), with coefficient sizes polynomially bounded on the admissible annulus.

Equivalently, after clearing powers of \(Y\), one may prove

\[
Y^M
=
U_9(Y)R_9(Y)
+
U_{11}(Y)P_{11}(0,Y)
+
U_{13}(Y)P_{13}(0,Y)
+\cdots
\]

in \(\mathbb C[Y]\).

On the annulus

\[
|Y|\ge Q^{-C_-},
\]

this gives

\[
1
\le
Q^{C}
\left(
|R_9(Y)|+|P_{11}(0,Y)|+|P_{13}(0,Y)|+\cdots
\right),
\]

hence

\[
|R_9(Y)|+|P_{11}(0,Y)|+|P_{13}(0,Y)|+\cdots
\ge Q^{-C'}.
\]

Multiplying by \(|Y|\) recovers a row lower bound.

So the finite D4 certificate is:

\[
\boxed{
\gcd_{\mathbb C[Y,Y^{-1}]}
\left(
R_9(Y),P_{11}(0,Y),P_{13}(0,Y),\ldots
\right)
=1.
}
\]

This is a one-variable gcd calculation.

---

# 4. Quantitative row lower bound from the certificate

Assume the Euclidean certificate has polynomial coefficient height:

\[
\|U_j\|\le Q^{C_U}.
\]

Assume

\[
Q^{-C_-}\le |Y|\le Q^{C_+}.
\]

Then

\[
|Y|^M
\le
\sum_j |U_j(Y)|\,|P_j(Y)|.
\]

Since

\[
|U_j(Y)|\le Q^{C}
\]

on the annulus, we get

\[
\max_j |P_j(Y)|
\ge Q^{-C'}.
\]

If the row is

\[
B_j=P_j(Y)+X(\cdots)+E_j,
\]

and

\[
|X|\le Q^{-K}|Y|
\]

with \(K\) large, then the \(X\)-dependent terms are perturbative.

If the errors satisfy

\[
|E_j|\le \frac12 Q^{-C'},
\]

then

\[
\max_j |B_j|\ge Q^{-C''}.
\]

Multiplying back by

\[
|H|\asymp |q_0||u|^3
\]

gives

\[
\max_j |A_j|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

at the D4 sample.

Thus the one-variable certificate produces the desired row lower bound.

---

# 5. What if the gcd is nontrivial?

Suppose

\[
D(Y)
=
\gcd\left(R_9(Y),P_{11}(0,Y),P_{13}(0,Y),\ldots\right)
\]

has a nonzero root

\[
\zeta\ne0.
\]

Then the known rows vanish at

\[
X=0,\qquad Y=\zeta.
\]

This is a genuine secondary anisotropy wall.

There are three attacks.

## 5.1 Add rows

Compute

\[
P_{15}(0,Y),\quad P_{17}(0,Y),\ldots
\]

until the gcd becomes \(1\) after localizing at \(Y\ne0\).

Since the row tower is expected to generate successive anisotropy responses, this should terminate unless the row architecture is intrinsically blind at that root.

## 5.2 Root escape

If \(Y(m)\) is near \(\zeta\), but

\[
|Y'(m)|\ge Q^{-C},
\]

then move nearby until

\[
|Y-\zeta|\ge Q^{-C'}
\]

while \(q_0,u,\Gamma\) remain live.

Then \(D(Y)\) is non-small and row conversion works.

This is the same first-exit logic used for the \(q_0\)-wall.

## 5.3 Modify Gate 2 anisotropy

If all rows vanish on a nonzero anisotropy state

\[
(X,Y)=(0,\zeta),
\]

then Gate 2 is measuring a direction invisible to the row architecture. In that case either:

- Gate 2 should be redefined using row-observed anisotropy combinations; or
- a different row family is required.

---

# 6. A likely simplification: \(R_9\) is constant

In the expected linear row structure,

\[
\widetilde B_9
=
c_9Y+\text{higher},
\]

so

\[
R_9(Y)=c_9.
\]

If

\[
c_9\ne0,
\]

then the gcd certificate is immediate:

\[
1=c_9^{-1}R_9(Y).
\]

No extra rows are needed.

This matches the originally displayed asymptotic

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Thus the whole one-variable audit likely collapses to checking

\[
c_9\ne0.
\]

---

# 7. Final D4 finite audit hierarchy

The exact audit hierarchy is now:

## Level 0 — Linear diagonal

Check

\[
A_7/H=c_7X+\text{higher},
\]

\[
A_9/H=c_9Y+\text{higher},
\]

with

\[
c_7c_9\ne0.
\]

If yes, D4 closes.

## Level 1 — Triangular nonlinear

After eliminating \(X\),

\[
\widetilde A_9/H
=
Y R_9(Y)+X(\cdots)+\text{error}.
\]

Check

\[
R_9(Y)
\]

has no admissible nonzero roots. If yes, D4 closes.

## Level 2 — Extra row root kill

If \(R_9\) has nonzero roots, compute

\[
P_{11}(0,Y),P_{13}(0,Y),\ldots
\]

and prove the localized gcd is \(1\).

## Level 3 — Escape or architecture defect

If a nonzero common root remains, use \(Y\)-root escape or declare a row-architecture defect.

---

# 8. Attack conclusion

D4 has been reduced to a one-variable Euclidean algorithm after triangularization.

The dangerous branch is no longer broad:

\[
\Gamma_G\approx0,\quad \Gamma_{G,2}\ne0,
\]

and all rows vanish.

It is captured by

\[
\gcd_{\mathbb C[Y,Y^{-1}]}
\left(R_9(Y),P_{11}(0,Y),P_{13}(0,Y),\ldots\right).
\]

If this gcd is \(1\), D4 closes.

Expected simplest outcome:

\[
R_9(Y)=c_9\ne0.
\]

Then D4 closes with \(A_7,A_9\) alone.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 11:38:14)

## Continuing attack — force the one-variable D4 audit into a concrete stopping criterion

The D4 row-conversion problem is now localized to a triangular chart:

\[
X=\Gamma_G,\qquad Y=\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

After using \(A_7\) to control \(X\), the only possible row-conversion failure occurs on

\[
X\approx0,\qquad Y\ne0.
\]

After triangularizing \(A_9\), the relevant expression has the form

\[
\widetilde A_9/H
=
Y R_9(Y)+X(\cdots)+E_9.
\]

So the bad branch is

\[
R_9(Y)\approx0
\]

for admissible nonzero \(Y\). I’ll now give the exact stopping criterion for this branch.

---

# 1. The one-variable stopping criterion

Let the triangularized row family restricted to \(X=0\) be

\[
P_9(Y):=\frac{\widetilde A_9}{HY}\bigg|_{X=0},
\]

\[
P_{11}(Y):=\frac{\widetilde A_{11}}{H}\bigg|_{X=0},
\]

\[
P_{13}(Y):=\frac{\widetilde A_{13}}{H}\bigg|_{X=0},
\]

and so on, with any harmless powers of \(Y\) divided out after localizing at \(Y\ne0\).

The exact finite stopping condition is

\[
\boxed{
\gcd_{\mathbb C[Y,Y^{-1}]}
\left(P_9(Y),P_{11}(Y),P_{13}(Y),\ldots,P_{2N+1}(Y)\right)
=1.
}
\]

Equivalently, there exists \(M\ge0\) and polynomials \(U_j(Y)\) such that

\[
\boxed{
Y^M=
U_9(Y)P_9(Y)
+
U_{11}(Y)P_{11}(Y)
+
U_{13}(Y)P_{13}(Y)
+\cdots+
U_{2N+1}(Y)P_{2N+1}(Y).
}
\]

On the admissible annulus

\[
Q^{-C_-}\le |Y|\le Q^{C_+},
\]

this gives the quantitative lower bound

\[
\max_j |P_j(Y)|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

provided the coefficient heights of \(U_j\) are polynomially bounded.

Thus the D4 secondary wall is eliminated by a one-variable Euclidean certificate.

---

# 2. Why \(Y=0\) is excluded

The localization at \(Y\ne0\) is legitimate.

On the branch

\[
X=\Gamma_G\approx0,
\]

if also

\[
Y=\Gamma_{G,2}\approx0,
\]

then

\[
|\Gamma|^2=X^2+Y^2\approx0.
\]

But Gate 2 pointwise mass requires

\[
|u|^2|\Gamma|^2\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

With polynomial upper bounds on \(u\), this forces

\[
|\Gamma|\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

Thus the row-conversion branch never needs to control \(Y=0\) after \(X\) has been made small. We may invert \(Y\).

---

# 3. Quantitative Euclidean certificate

Assume the certificate

\[
Y^M=\sum_j U_j(Y)P_j(Y)
\]

has degree and coefficient-height bounds

\[
\deg U_j\le d,\qquad \|U_j\|\le Q^{C_U}.
\]

On

\[
|Y|\le Q^{C_+},
\]

we have

\[
|U_j(Y)|\le Q^{C_U+dC_+}.
\]

Also

\[
|Y^M|\ge Q^{-MC_-}.
\]

Hence

\[
Q^{-MC_-}
\le
\sum_j Q^{C_U+dC_+}|P_j(Y)|.
\]

Therefore

\[
\max_j |P_j(Y)|
\ge
Q^{-MC_- - C_U-dC_+-O(1)}.
\]

This is the needed polynomial row-symbol lower bound.

Multiplying back by

\[
|H|\,|Y|
\]

gives row lower bound. Since

\[
|H|\asymp |q_0||u|^3
\]

and \(Y\) is polynomially nonzero, D4 closes.

---

# 4. The expected immediate close

In the displayed row architecture,

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

After triangularization, this means

\[
\widetilde A_9/H
=
c_9Y+\text{higher}.
\]

If no pure nonlinear \(Y\)-terms occur at the same principal order, then

\[
P_9(Y)=c_9.
\]

If

\[
c_9\ne0,
\]

the certificate is trivial:

\[
1=c_9^{-1}P_9(Y).
\]

Then the one-variable branch closes with \(A_9\) alone.

Even if pure \(Y\)-terms occur,

\[
P_9(Y)=c_9+d_1Y+d_2Y^2+\cdots,
\]

the branch closes if \(P_9\) has no nonzero admissible root. If it does have roots, add rows and run the gcd test.

---

# 5. If \(P_9\) has nonzero roots

Suppose

\[
P_9(\zeta)=0
\]

for some

\[
\zeta\ne0.
\]

Then at

\[
X=0,\qquad Y=\zeta,
\]

rows \(A_7\) and triangularized \(A_9\) vanish.

Add \(A_{11}\). Compute

\[
P_{11}(\zeta).
\]

If

\[
P_{11}(\zeta)\ne0
\]

for every nonzero admissible root \(\zeta\), D4 closes.

Equivalently,

\[
\gcd_{\mathbb C[Y,Y^{-1}]}(P_9,P_{11})=1.
\]

If not, add \(P_{13}\), etc.

This is finite.

---

# 6. If all rows share a nonzero root

If for some \(\zeta\ne0\),

\[
P_j(\zeta)=0
\qquad
\forall j
\]

for all known rows, then the row architecture is blind to the anisotropy state

\[
\Gamma_G=0,\qquad \Gamma_{G,2}=\zeta.
\]

Then one of three things must happen:

1. add a new row family;
2. modify Gate 2 anisotropy to avoid the invisible direction;
3. use root-escape sampling if \(Y(m)\) is not trapped near \(\zeta\).

But this is now an extremely specific defect, not a broad D4 uncertainty.

---

# 7. Root-escape fallback

If \(Y\) is near a nonzero common root \(\zeta\), define

\[
S(m)=Y(m)-\zeta.
\]

If

\[
|S(m_0)|\ll1
\]

but

\[
|S'(m_0)|\ge Q^{-C}
\]

or

\[
|S''(m_0)|\ge Q^{-C},
\]

then by first-exit sampling there is a nearby point with

\[
|Y-\zeta|\ge Q^{-C'}
\]

while \(q_0,u,\Gamma\) remain live. Then \(P_j(Y)\) becomes non-small for some \(j\), and D4 closes.

So even a finite common-root obstruction may be escapable under existential sampling.

---

# 8. Final D4 decision tree

The D4 audit now has a deterministic decision tree:

1. **Check \(A_7\):**

   \[
   A_7/H=c_7X+\cdots,\qquad c_7\ne0.
   \]

   If false, replace \(A_7\) with another row that detects \(X\).

2. **Triangularize \(A_9\):**

   \[
   \widetilde A_9=A_9-\beta A_7.
   \]

3. **Restrict to \(X=0\), localize at \(Y\ne0\):**

   \[
   \widetilde A_9/H=Y P_9(Y)+\cdots.
   \]

4. **If \(P_9\) is a nonzero constant, stop.**

5. **If \(P_9\) has nonzero roots, add \(P_{11},P_{13},\ldots\).**

6. **Run Euclidean gcd:**

   \[
   \gcd_{\mathbb C[Y,Y^{-1}]}(P_9,P_{11},P_{13},\ldots)=1.
   \]

7. **If gcd is \(1\), D4 closes.**

8. **If gcd has nonzero root, use root-escape or declare row-architecture defect.**

This is now an exact finite procedure.

---

# 9. Attack conclusion

The D4 row conversion problem has been reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a one-variable Euclidean algorithm in }Y=\Gamma_{G,2}
\text{ after triangularization by }A_7.
}
\]

Expected simplest certificate:

\[
\boxed{
P_9(Y)=c_9\ne0.
}
\]

If confirmed, D4 closes with \(A_7,A_9\) alone.

If not, the finite gcd over \(P_9,P_{11},P_{13},\ldots\) gives a precise stopping criterion or exposes the exact invisible anisotropy state.

## Assistant (2026-04-27 11:39:00)

I’ll continue from the one-variable D4 audit. The next useful attack is to handle the only remaining bad case: a nonzero common root of the triangularized row polynomials.

_Thought for 23s_

## Continuing attack — kill nonzero D4 root traps by ODE rigidity

We have reduced the hardest D4 row-conversion branch to a one-variable triangular chart:

\[
X=\Gamma_G,\qquad Y=\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

After \(A_7\) controls \(X\), the only possible remaining D4 failure is

\[
X\approx0,\qquad Y\ne0,\qquad P_j(Y)\approx0
\]

for all triangularized higher rows. The previous finite-algebra audit says: if the localized gcd in \(Y\) is \(1\), D4 closes. Now I’ll attack the bad case where the gcd has a **nonzero common root**.

The key point: a nonzero root trap would require

\[
\Gamma_G\approx0,\qquad \Gamma_{G,2}\approx \zeta\ne0
\]

over a sampling interval. But the identities defining \(\Gamma_G,\Gamma_{G,2}\) appear to forbid a stable interval with \(X=0\) and \(Y=\zeta\ne0\). This gives a new ODE-rigidity escape from the residual D4 root branch. The downstream setting and row-conversion context are the same as in the latest handoff: Gate 2 plus Wall-Escape Sampling plus D4 matrix/row conversion. 

---

# 1. Exact root-trap equations

Recall

\[
X=\Gamma_G=q''-6q+2q^3,
\]

and

\[
Y=\Gamma_{G,2}=2q(q')^2-\frac16X''.
\]

If

\[
X\equiv0
\]

on an interval, then

\[
q''=6q-2q^3.
\]

Also

\[
X''\equiv0,
\]

so

\[
Y=2q(q')^2.
\]

The residual row bad branch after triangularization is approximately

\[
X=0,\qquad Y=\zeta,
\]

where \(\zeta\ne0\) is a common root of the one-variable row polynomials.

So the exact trapped branch would satisfy

\[
q''=6q-2q^3,
\]

\[
2q(q')^2=\zeta\ne0.
\]

I will show this is impossible over a real interval.

---

# 2. Exact impossibility of nonzero constant \(Y\)

Assume

\[
q''=6q-2q^3,
\]

and

\[
2q(q')^2=\zeta
\]

with \(\zeta\ne0\) constant on an interval.

Differentiate

\[
2q(q')^2=\zeta.
\]

We get

\[
2q'(q')^2+4qq'q''=0,
\]

or

\[
2q'\left((q')^2+2qq''\right)=0.
\]

If \(q'\equiv0\) on a subinterval, then

\[
Y=2q(q')^2=0,
\]

contradicting \(\zeta\ne0\).

So on a subinterval where \(q'\ne0\), we must have

\[
(q')^2+2qq''=0.
\]

Substitute

\[
q''=6q-2q^3.
\]

Then

\[
(q')^2+12q^2-4q^4=0,
\]

so

\[
(q')^2=4q^4-12q^2=4q^2(q^2-3).
\]

Differentiate this identity:

\[
2q'q''
=
(16q^3-24q)q'.
\]

Since \(q'\ne0\),

\[
q''=8q^3-12q.
\]

But also

\[
q''=6q-2q^3.
\]

Therefore

\[
6q-2q^3=8q^3-12q,
\]

so

\[
18q-10q^3=0,
\]

hence

\[
q(9-5q^2)=0.
\]

Since \(\zeta\ne0\), \(q\ne0\), so

\[
q^2=\frac95.
\]

But then

\[
(q')^2=4q^2(q^2-3)
=
4\cdot\frac95\left(\frac95-3\right)
=
\frac{36}{5}\cdot\left(-\frac65\right)
<0,
\]

impossible for real \(q\).

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
X\equiv0,\quad Y\equiv\zeta\ne0
\text{ is impossible on a real interval.}
}
\]

This is a strong rigidity statement.

---

# 3. Meaning for the D4 root branch

The triangular D4 failure branch was:

\[
X\approx0,
\qquad
Y\approx \zeta\ne0,
\]

where \(\zeta\) is a nonzero common root of the row-polynomial gcd.

If \(X,Y-\zeta\) were small on a whole interval, the exact rigidity above says this is unstable. Quantitatively, one should get:

\[
\sup_J |X|+\sup_J|Y-\zeta|\le \varepsilon
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
|J|\le Q^C\varepsilon^c
\]

unless \(\zeta\) is itself tiny.

But \(\zeta\ne0\) and the Gate 2 branch localizes away from \(Y=0\):

\[
|Y|\ge Q^{-C}.
\]

So the nonzero-root branch cannot occupy a macroscopic interval.

This gives an alternative to adding rows:

\[
\boxed{
\text{nonzero D4 row roots are dynamically unstable under the }(X,Y)\text{ ODE identities.}
}
\]

---

# 4. Quantitative root-trap rigidity target

The proof-level theorem should be:

## Nonzero Root-Trap Rigidity

Let \(\zeta\) satisfy

\[
|\zeta|\ge Q^{-C_\zeta}.
\]

Assume on an interval \(J\):

\[
|X|=|\Gamma_G|\le \varepsilon,
\]

\[
|Y-\zeta|=|\Gamma_{G,2}-\zeta|\le \varepsilon,
\]

and polynomial derivative bounds

\[
\max_{k\le 4}|q^{(k)}|\le Q^C.
\]

Then for a shrunken interval \(J'\subset J\),

\[
|J'|\le Q^C\varepsilon^c
\]

or, equivalently, if \(|J'|\ge Q^{-C}\), then

\[
\varepsilon\ge Q^{-C'}.
\]

This means the root-trap set cannot support the kind of polynomial interval sample required by Gate 2 extraction and row mobility.

---

# 5. How to prove quantitative rigidity

Use the exact algebraic contradiction above in a stability form.

Let

\[
X=q''-6q+2q^3.
\]

If

\[
|X|\le\varepsilon,
\]

then

\[
q''=6q-2q^3+O(\varepsilon).
\]

Also

\[
Y=2q(q')^2-\frac16X''.
\]

The difficulty is \(X''\). But in the D4 row-root branch we are not necessarily in the Gate 2 integral-rigidity setting. To get interval control, we need either:

1. \(X''\) small, or  
2. integrate by parts as in the Gate 2 integral-rigidity lemma, or  
3. use \(Y-\zeta\) small plus derivative bounds to control \(X''\) weakly.

The cleanest route is an **integral version**:

Assume

\[
\int_J |X|^2+|Y-\zeta|^2\le \varepsilon^2|J|.
\]

Then use the same integration-by-parts trick from Gate 2 rigidity to avoid needing \(X''\) pointwise.

This suggests the root-trap branch can be killed by the same machinery being developed for Gate 2.

---

# 6. Integral root-trap rigidity

A suitable theorem:

## Integral Nonzero Root-Trap Rigidity

Let

\[
X=\Gamma_G,\qquad Y=\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

For every \(|\zeta|\ge Q^{-C}\), there is \(c>0\) such that

\[
\int_J |X|^2+|Y-\zeta|^2\,dm
\le \varepsilon^2|J|
\]

implies

\[
\int_{J'} |Y|^2\,dm
\le Q^C\varepsilon^c|J'|
\]

or forces

\[
|J'|
\le Q^C\varepsilon^c|J|.
\]

But on the root branch, \(Y\approx\zeta\ne0\), so

\[
\int_{J'}|Y|^2\ge Q^{-C}|J'|.
\]

Contradiction for \(\varepsilon\) sufficiently small.

Thus nonzero root traps cannot persist on intervals.

---

# 7. Consequence for D4 sampling

If the row-polynomial \(P_9(Y)\) has a nonzero root \(\zeta\), the D4 bad set near that root is

\[
X\approx0,\qquad Y\approx\zeta.
\]

There are three possibilities:

## Case 1 — only isolated points

Then row mobility/existential sampling avoids them. They cannot carry row-energy obstruction.

## Case 2 — interval trap

Impossible by root-trap rigidity.

## Case 3 — finite but sampled exactly

If the downstream proof were exact-point locked, this could matter. But row conversion is now existential/energetic, so exact isolated points are harmless.

Therefore, even if \(P_9\) has nonzero roots, they may not obstruct D4 under existential sampling.

This is stronger than the previous finite gcd approach.

---

# 8. Updated D4 root branch closure

We can now state:

## D4 Nonzero Root Escape/Rigidity Theorem

Suppose after triangularization,

\[
B_7=X+E_7,
\]

\[
B_9=Y R_Y(Y)+X(\cdots)+E_9.
\]

Let \(\zeta\ne0\) be a root of \(R_Y\). If row conversion is energetic/existential and the local dynamics obey the root-trap rigidity theorem, then the branch

\[
X\approx0,\qquad Y\approx\zeta
\]

does not obstruct D4. It cannot persist on an interval carrying Gate 2 mass, and isolated occurrences can be avoided by sampling.

Thus D4 closes provided:

1. \(A_7\) controls \(X\);
2. \(A_9\) controls \(Y\) except finite nonzero roots;
3. nonzero root traps are dynamically unstable;
4. row sampling is existential.

---

# 9. What remains after this attack?

The only unresolved D4 cases are now:

## A. \(Y=0\)

But this is not a Gate 2 branch if \(X=0\), since \(\Gamma=0\).

## B. Nonzero root trap over an interval

Ruled out by exact ODE rigidity and targeted quantitative/integral rigidity.

## C. Exact-point lock

Already identified as an architectural issue. If row sampling is existential, no issue.

## D. Row-polynomial roots depending on \(Y\)-other variables

If the root \(\zeta\) is not constant but depends on local variables \(Y_{\rm loc}\), then the root-trap equation becomes

\[
Y\approx \zeta(Y_{\rm loc}).
\]

This needs a more general rigidity/escape statement, but still finite: define

\[
S(m)=Y(m)-\zeta(Y_{\rm loc}(m))
\]

and use escape unless \(S\) is trapped on an interval, in which case differentiate and seek contradiction.

---

# 10. Attack conclusion

The one-variable D4 gcd obstruction is weaker than it looked.

Even if triangularized \(A_9\) has nonzero roots in \(Y\), a persistent bad branch would require

\[
\Gamma_G\approx0,\qquad \Gamma_{G,2}\approx\zeta\ne0
\]

on an interval.

But the exact equations imply this is impossible for real trajectories.

Thus D4 can likely be closed by:

\[
\boxed{
\text{triangular row structure}
+
\text{nonzero root-trap rigidity}
+
\text{existential row sampling}.
}
\]

The remaining proof work is to make the nonzero root-trap rigidity quantitative, likely using the same integration-by-parts machinery as Gate 2 integral rigidity.
